<p><b>Nelson Rolihlahla Mandela</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <small>Xhosa: </small><span class="IPA" lang="xh-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Nguni" title="Help:IPA/Nguni">[xolíɬaɬa mandɛ̂ːla]</a></span>; 18 July 1918 – 5 December 2013) was a South African <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">anti-apartheid</a> activist who served as the <a href="/wiki/President_of_South_Africa" title="President of South Africa">first president of South Africa</a> from 1994 to 1999. He was the country's first black head of state and the first elected in a <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">fully representative</a> democratic election. <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Nelson_Mandela" title="Presidency of Nelson Mandela">His government</a> focused on dismantling the legacy of <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> by fostering racial <a href="/wiki/Conflict_resolution" title="Conflict resolution">reconciliation</a>. Ideologically an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_nationalist" title="African nationalist">African nationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_socialism" title="African socialism">socialist</a>, he served as the president of the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) party from 1991 to 1997.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a>, Mandela was born into the <a href="/wiki/Thembu_people" title="Thembu people">Thembu</a> royal family in <a href="/wiki/Mvezo" title="Mvezo">Mvezo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a>. He studied law at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Fort_Hare" title="University of Fort Hare">University of Fort Hare</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Witwatersrand" title="University of Witwatersrand">University of Witwatersrand</a> before working as a lawyer in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>. There he became involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-colonial" title="Anti-colonial">anti-colonial</a> and African nationalist politics, joining the ANC in 1943 and co-founding its <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress_Youth_League" title="African National Congress Youth League">Youth League</a> in 1944. After the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Dominant_minority" title="Dominant minority">white-only government</a> established apartheid, a system of <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> that privileged <a href="/wiki/White_South_Africans" title="White South Africans">whites</a>, Mandela and the ANC committed themselves to its overthrow. He was appointed president of the ANC's <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_(province)" title="Transvaal (province)">Transvaal</a> branch, rising to prominence for his involvement in the 1952 <a href="/wiki/Defiance_Campaign" title="Defiance Campaign">Defiance Campaign</a> and the 1955 <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_People_(1955)" title="Congress of the People (1955)">Congress of the People</a>. He was repeatedly arrested for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seditious" title="Seditious">seditious</a> activities and was unsuccessfully prosecuted in the <a href="/wiki/1956_Treason_Trial" title="1956 Treason Trial">1956 Treason Trial</a>. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, he secretly joined the banned <a href="/wiki/South_African_Communist_Party" title="South African Communist Party">South African Communist Party</a> (SACP). Although initially committed to non-violent protest, in association with the SACP he co-founded the militant <a href="/wiki/UMkhonto_we_Sizwe" title="UMkhonto we Sizwe">uMkhonto we Sizwe</a> in 1961 and led a <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">sabotage</a> campaign against the government. He was arrested and imprisoned in 1962, and, following the <a href="/wiki/Rivonia_Trial" title="Rivonia Trial">Rivonia Trial</a>, was sentenced to life imprisonment for conspiring to overthrow the state.
</p><p>Mandela served 27 years in prison, split between <a href="/wiki/Robben_Island" title="Robben Island">Robben Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollsmoor_Prison" title="Pollsmoor Prison">Pollsmoor Prison</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Verster_Prison" title="Victor Verster Prison">Victor Verster Prison</a>. Amid growing domestic and international pressure and fears of racial civil war, President <a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">F. W. de Klerk</a> released him in 1990. Mandela and de Klerk led efforts to negotiate an end to apartheid, which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/1994_South_African_general_election" title="1994 South African general election">1994 multiracial general election</a> in which Mandela led the ANC to victory and became president. Leading a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(South_Africa)" title="Government of National Unity (South Africa)">broad coalition government</a> which promulgated a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">new constitution</a>, Mandela emphasised reconciliation between the country's racial groups and created the <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission_(South_Africa)" title="Truth and Reconciliation Commission (South Africa)">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</a> to investigate past <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_South_Africa" title="Human rights in South Africa">human rights</a> abuses. Economically, his administration retained its predecessor's <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">liberal framework</a> despite his own socialist beliefs, also introducing measures to encourage <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">combat poverty</a> and expand healthcare services. Internationally, Mandela acted as mediator in the <a href="/wiki/Pan_Am_Flight_103_bombing_trial" title="Pan Am Flight 103 bombing trial">Pan Am Flight 103 bombing trial</a> and served as secretary-general of the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> from 1998 to 1999. He declined a second presidential term and was succeeded by his deputy, <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a>. Mandela became an elder statesman and focused on combating poverty and <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> through the charitable <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela_Foundation" title="Nelson Mandela Foundation">Nelson Mandela Foundation</a>.
</p><p>Mandela was a controversial figure for much of his life. Although critics on <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">the right</a> denounced him as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist_terrorist" title="Communist terrorist">communist terrorist</a> and those on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_left" title="Far left">far left</a> deemed him too eager to negotiate and reconcile with apartheid's supporters, he gained international acclaim for his activism. Globally regarded as an icon of democracy and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>, he received <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_honours_received_by_Nelson_Mandela" title="List of awards and honours received by Nelson Mandela">more than 250 honours</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>. He is held in deep respect within South Africa, where he is often referred to by his <a href="/wiki/Thembu_people" title="Thembu people">Thembu</a> <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_clan_names" title="Xhosa clan names">clan name</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madiba" title="Madiba">Madiba</a>, and described as the "<a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation" title="Father of the Nation">Father of the Nation</a>".
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>
<h4><span id="Childhood:_1918.E2.80.931934"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood:_1918–1934">Childhood: 1918–1934</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mandela_family" title="Mandela family">Mandela family</a></div>
<p>Mandela was born on 18 July 1918 in the village of Mvezo in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umtata" title="Umtata">Umtata</a>, then part of South Africa's <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela19943Boehmer200821Smith201017Sampson20113_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela19943Boehmer200821Smith201017Sampson20113-2">[2]</a></sup> Given the forename Rolihlahla,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 1]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a> term colloquially meaning "troublemaker",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198616Mandela19943Smith201017Meredith20102Sampson20113_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198616Mandela19943Smith201017Meredith20102Sampson20113-5">[4]</a></sup> in later years he became known by his clan name, Madiba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela19944Lodge20062Smith201016_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela19944Lodge20062Smith201016-6">[5]</a></sup> His patrilineal great-grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Ngubengcuka" title="Ngubengcuka">Ngubengcuka</a>, was ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Thembu_people" title="Thembu people">Thembu Kingdom</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkeian</a> Territories of South Africa's modern <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Cape" title="Eastern Cape">Eastern Cape</a> province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer19883GuiloineauRowe200223Meredith20101_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer19883GuiloineauRowe200223Meredith20101-7">[6]</a></sup> One of Ngubengcuka's sons, named Mandela, was Nelson's grandfather and the source of his surname.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuiloineauRowe200226_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuiloineauRowe200226-8">[7]</a></sup> Because Mandela was the king's child by a wife of the Ixhiba clan, a so-called "Left-Hand House", the descendants of his <a href="/wiki/Cadet_branch" title="Cadet branch">cadet branch</a> of the royal family were <a href="/wiki/Morganatic_marriage" title="Morganatic marriage">morganatic</a>, ineligible to inherit the throne but recognised as hereditary royal councillors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuiloineauRowe200226Lodge20061Mafela2008102–103_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuiloineauRowe200226Lodge20061Mafela2008102–103-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nelson Mandela's father, Gadla Henry Mphakanyiswa Mandela (1880–1928), was a local chief and councillor to the monarch; he was appointed to the position in 1915, after his predecessor was accused of corruption by a governing white magistrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith201019_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith201019-10">[9]</a></sup> In 1926, Gadla was also sacked for corruption, but Nelson was told that his father had lost his job for standing up to the magistrate's unreasonable demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela19948–9Smith201021–22Sampson20114_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela19948–9Smith201021–22Sampson20114-11">[10]</a></sup> A devotee of the god <a href="/wiki/Qamata" title="Qamata">Qamata</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199417Meredith20101_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199417Meredith20101-12">[11]</a></sup> Gadla was a polygamist with four wives, four sons and nine daughters, who lived in different villages. Nelson's mother was Gadla's third wife, Nosekeni Fanny, daughter of Nkedama of the Right Hand House and a member of the amaMpemvu clan of the Xhosa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198615Mandela19947–8Smith201016,_23–24Meredith20101,_3Sampson20114_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198615Mandela19947–8Smith201016,_23–24Meredith20101,_3Sampson20114-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>No one in <a href="/wiki/Mandela_family" title="Mandela family">my family</a> had ever attended school ... On the first day of school my teacher, Miss Mdingane, gave each of us an English name. This was the custom among Africans in those days and was undoubtedly due to the British bias of our education. That day, Miss Mdingane told me that my new name was Nelson. Why this particular name, I have no idea.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Mandela, 1994<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199419_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199419-14">[13]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Mandela later stated that his early life was dominated by traditional Xhosa custom and taboo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199415Meredith20103_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199415Meredith20103-15">[14]</a></sup> He grew up with two sisters in his mother's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homestead_(small_African_settlement)" title="Homestead (small African settlement)">kraal</a></i> in the village of <a href="/wiki/Qunu" title="Qunu">Qunu</a>, where he tended herds as a cattle-boy and spent much time outside with other boys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198616Mandela199412Smith201023–24Meredith20102,_4_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198616Mandela199412Smith201023–24Meredith20102,_4-16">[15]</a></sup> Both his parents were illiterate, but his mother, being a devout Christian, sent him to a local <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodist</a> school when he was about seven. Baptised a Methodist, Mandela was given the English forename of "Nelson" by his teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199418–19Lodge20063Smith201024Meredith20102,_4–5Sampson20115,_7Forster201491–92_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199418–19Lodge20063Smith201024Meredith20102,_4–5Sampson20115,_7Forster201491–92-17">[16]</a></sup> When Mandela was about nine, his father came to stay at Qunu, where he died of an undiagnosed ailment that Mandela believed to be lung disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199420Lodge20063Smith201025Meredith20105Sampson20117_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199420Lodge20063Smith201025Meredith20105Sampson20117-18">[17]</a></sup> Feeling "cut adrift", he later said that he inherited his father's "proud rebelliousness" and "stubborn sense of fairness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela19948,_20_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela19948,_20-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela's mother took him to the "Great Place" palace at Mqhekezweni, where he was entrusted to the guardianship of the Thembu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regency_(government)" title="Regency (government)">regent</a>, Chief Jongintaba Dalindyebo. Although he did not see his mother again for many years, Mandela felt that Jongintaba and his wife Noengland treated him as their own child, raising him alongside their son, Justice, and daughter, Nomafu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198617Meer19884Mandela199422–25Lodge20063Smith201026–27Meredith20105Sampson20117–9_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198617Meer19884Mandela199422–25Lodge20063Smith201026–27Meredith20105Sampson20117–9-20">[19]</a></sup> As Mandela attended church services every Sunday with his guardians, Christianity became a significant part of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer19887Mandela199427–29Meredith20108–9_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer19887Mandela199427–29Meredith20108–9-21">[20]</a></sup> He attended a Methodist mission school located next to the palace, where he studied English, Xhosa, history and geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer19887Mandela199425Smith201027Meredith20109_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer19887Mandela199425Smith201027Meredith20109-22">[21]</a></sup> He developed a love of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_history" title="African history">African history</a>, listening to the tales told by elderly visitors to the palace, and was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Anti-imperialism" title="Anti-imperialism">anti-imperialist</a> rhetoric of a visiting chief, Joyi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198811–12Mandela199431–34Lodge20063Smith201018Meredith20108_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198811–12Mandela199431–34Lodge20063Smith201018Meredith20108-23">[22]</a></sup> Nevertheless, at the time he considered the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">European colonizers</a> not as oppressors but as benefactors who had brought education and other benefits to southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199443Meredith201011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199443Meredith201011-24">[23]</a></sup> Aged 16, he, Justice and several other boys travelled to Tyhalarha to undergo the <a href="/wiki/Ulwaluko" title="Ulwaluko">ulwaluko</a> <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a> ritual that symbolically marked their transition from boys to men; afterwards he was given the name <i>Dalibunga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198617Mandela199436–42Lodge20068Smith201029–31Meredith20109–11Sampson201114_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198617Mandela199436–42Lodge20068Smith201029–31Meredith20109–11Sampson201114-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Clarkebury.2C_Healdtown.2C_and_Fort_Hare:_1934.E2.80.931940"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Clarkebury,_Healdtown,_and_Fort_Hare:_1934–1940">Clarkebury, Healdtown, and Fort Hare: 1934–1940</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Mandela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1308" data-file-width="955" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Young_Mandela.jpg/170px-Young_Mandela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Young_Mandela.jpg/255px-Young_Mandela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Young_Mandela.jpg/340px-Young_Mandela.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Mandela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of Mandela, taken in Umtata in 1937</div></div></div>
<p>Intending to gain skills needed to become a <a href="/wiki/Privy_council" title="Privy council">privy councillor</a> for the Thembu royal house, Mandela began his secondary education in 1933 at <a href="/wiki/Clarkebury" title="Clarkebury">Clarkebury</a> Methodist High School in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engcobo" title="Engcobo">Engcobo</a>, a Western-style institution that was the largest school for black Africans in <a href="/wiki/Thembuland" title="Thembuland">Thembuland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199445–47Smith201027,_31Meredith201012–13Sampson201115_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199445–47Smith201027,_31Meredith201012–13Sampson201115-26">[25]</a></sup> Made to socialise with other students on an equal basis, he claimed that he lost his "stuck up" attitude, becoming best friends with a girl for the first time; he began playing sports and developed his lifelong love of gardening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199448–50_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199448–50-27">[26]</a></sup> He completed his Junior Certificate in two years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson201117_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson201117-28">[27]</a></sup> and in 1937 he moved to <a href="/wiki/Healdtown_Comprehensive_School" title="Healdtown Comprehensive School">Healdtown</a>, the Methodist college in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Beaufort" title="Fort Beaufort">Fort Beaufort</a> attended by most Thembu royalty, including Justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199452Smith201031–32Meredith201014Sampson201117–18_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199452Smith201031–32Meredith201014Sampson201117–18-29">[28]</a></sup> The headmaster emphasised the superiority of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Europe" title="Culture of Europe">European culture</a> and government, but Mandela became increasingly interested in native <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Africa" title="Culture of Africa">African culture</a>, making his first non-Xhosa friend, a speaker of <a href="/wiki/Sotho_language" title="Sotho language">Sotho</a>, and coming under the influence of one of his favourite teachers, a Xhosa who broke taboo by marrying a Sotho.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199453–54Smith201032Meredith201014–15Sampson201118–21_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199453–54Smith201032Meredith201014–15Sampson201118–21-30">[29]</a></sup> Mandela spent much of his spare time at Healdtown as a long-distance runner and boxer, and in his second year he became a <a href="/wiki/Prefect" title="Prefect">prefect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199456Smith201032Meredith201015_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199456Smith201032Meredith201015-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1939, with Jongintaba's backing, Mandela began work on a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">BA degree</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Fort_Hare" title="University of Fort Hare">University of Fort Hare</a>, an elite black institution of approximately 150 students in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alice,_Eastern_Cape" title="Alice, Eastern Cape">Alice, Eastern Cape</a>. He studied English, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, politics, "native administration", and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Dutch_law" title="Roman Dutch law">Roman Dutch law</a> in his first year, desiring to become an interpreter or clerk in the <a href="/wiki/INTAF" title="INTAF">Native Affairs Department</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199462–65Lodge20069Smith201033–34Meredith201015–18Sampson201121,_25_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199462–65Lodge20069Smith201033–34Meredith201015–18Sampson201121,_25-32">[31]</a></sup> Mandela stayed in the Wesley House dormitory, befriending his own kinsman, <a href="/wiki/Kaiser_Matanzima" title="Kaiser Matanzima">K. D. Matanzima</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Tambo" title="Oliver Tambo">Oliver Tambo</a>, who became a close friend and comrade for decades to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199462–63Smith201033–34Meredith201017–19Sampson201124–25_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199462–63Smith201033–34Meredith201017–19Sampson201124–25-33">[32]</a></sup> He took up ballroom dancing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199467–69Smith201034Meredith201018Sampson201125_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199467–69Smith201034Meredith201018Sampson201125-34">[33]</a></sup> performed in a drama society play about <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199468Lodge200610Smith201035Meredith201018Sampson201125_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199468Lodge200610Smith201035Meredith201018Sampson201125-35">[34]</a></sup> and gave Bible classes in the local community as part of the Student Christian Association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199468Lodge200610Meredith201018Forster201493_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199468Lodge200610Meredith201018Forster201493-36">[35]</a></sup> Although he had friends who held connections to the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) who wanted South Africa to be independent of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, Mandela avoided any involvement with the nascent movement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson201125_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson201125-37">[36]</a></sup> and became a vocal supporter of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire_in_World_War_II" title="British Empire in World War II">British war effort</a> when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> broke out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199470–71Lodge200611Meredith201019Sampson201126_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199470–71Lodge200611Meredith201019Sampson201126-38">[37]</a></sup> He helped establish a first-year students' house committee which challenged the dominance of the second-years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199466Smith201034_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199466Smith201034-39">[38]</a></sup> and at the end of his first year became involved in a <a href="/wiki/Students%27_representative_council" title="Students' representative council">students' representative council</a> (SRC) boycott against the quality of food, for which he was suspended from the university; he never returned to complete his degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198621Mandela199478–86Lodge200611–12Smith201034–35Meredith201019–20Sampson201126–27_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198621Mandela199478–86Lodge200611–12Smith201034–35Meredith201019–20Sampson201126–27-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Arriving_in_Johannesburg:_1941.E2.80.931943"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arriving_in_Johannesburg:_1941–1943">Arriving in Johannesburg: 1941–1943</span></h4>
<p>Returning to Mqhekezweni in December 1940, Mandela found that Jongintaba had <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriages</a> for him and Justice; dismayed, they fled to <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a> via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queenstown,_Eastern_Cape" title="Queenstown, Eastern Cape">Queenstown</a>, arriving in April 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198621Mandela199473–76Lodge200612Smith201036–39Meredith201020–22Sampson201127–28_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198621Mandela199473–76Lodge200612Smith201036–39Meredith201020–22Sampson201127–28-41">[40]</a></sup> Mandela found work as a night watchman at Crown Mines, his "first sight of South African capitalism in action", but was fired when the <i>induna</i> (headman) discovered that he was a runaway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198623Meer198825–26Mandela199489–94Lodge200612–13Smith201040Meredith201027–28Sampson201129–30_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198623Meer198825–26Mandela199489–94Lodge200612–13Smith201040Meredith201027–28Sampson201129–30-42">[41]</a></sup> He stayed with a cousin in George Goch Township, who introduced Mandela to realtor and ANC activist <a href="/wiki/Walter_Sisulu" title="Walter Sisulu">Walter Sisulu</a>. The latter secured Mandela a job as an <a href="/wiki/Articled_clerk" title="Articled clerk">articled clerk</a> at the law firm of Witkin, Sidelsky and Eidelman, a company run by Lazar Sidelsky, a liberal Jew sympathetic to the ANC's cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela199496–101Lodge200613,_19–21Smith201041Meredith201028–30Sampson201130–31_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela199496–101Lodge200613,_19–21Smith201041Meredith201028–30Sampson201130–31-43">[42]</a></sup> At the firm, Mandela befriended Gaur Radebe—a Hlubi member of the ANC and <a href="/wiki/South_African_Communist_Party" title="South African Communist Party">Communist Party</a>—and Nat Bregman, a Jewish <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> who became his first white friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994104–105Lodge200622,_31–32Smith201043,_48Meredith201031–32Sampson201132–33_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994104–105Lodge200622,_31–32Smith201043,_48Meredith201031–32Sampson201132–33-44">[43]</a></sup> Mandela attended Communist Party gatherings, where he was impressed that <a href="/wiki/White_South_Africans" title="White South Africans">Europeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Africans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_South_Africans" title="Asian South Africans">Indians</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coloured" title="Coloured">Coloureds</a> mixed as equals. He later stated that he did not join the party because its <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> conflicted with his Christian faith, and because he saw the South African struggle as being racially based rather than as <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class warfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994106Smith201048–49_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994106Smith201048–49-45">[44]</a></sup> To continue his higher education, Mandela signed up to a <a href="/wiki/University_of_South_Africa" title="University of South Africa">University of South Africa</a> correspondence course, working on his bachelor's degree at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994100Smith201044Meredith201033Sampson201134_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994100Smith201044Meredith201033Sampson201134-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earning a small wage, Mandela rented a room in the house of the Xhoma family in the <a href="/wiki/Alexandra,_Gauteng" title="Alexandra, Gauteng">Alexandra</a> township; despite being rife with poverty, crime and pollution, Alexandra always remained a special place for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198623Meer198826Mandela199499,_108–110Smith201044–45Meredith201033Sampson201133_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198623Meer198826Mandela199499,_108–110Smith201044–45Meredith201033Sampson201133-47">[46]</a></sup> Although embarrassed by his poverty, he briefly dated a <a href="/wiki/Swazi_people" title="Swazi people">Swazi</a> woman before unsuccessfully courting his landlord's daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994113–116Lodge200623Smith201045–46Sampson201133_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994113–116Lodge200623Smith201045–46Sampson201133-48">[47]</a></sup> To save money and be closer to downtown Johannesburg, Mandela moved into the compound of the <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand_Native_Labour_Association" title="Witwatersrand Native Labour Association">Witwatersrand Native Labour Association</a>, living among miners of various tribes; as the compound was visited by various chiefs, he once met the <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">Queen Regent</a> of <a href="/wiki/Basutoland" title="Basutoland">Basutoland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994118–119Lodge200624Meredith201033Sampson201134_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994118–119Lodge200624Meredith201033Sampson201134-49">[48]</a></sup> In late 1941, Jongintaba visited Johannesburg—there forgiving Mandela for running away—before returning to Thembuland, where he died in the winter of 1942. Mandela and Justice arrived a day late for the funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994116–117,_119–120Lodge200622Smith201047Meredith201033–34Sampson201133_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994116–117,_119–120Lodge200622Smith201047Meredith201033–34Sampson201133-50">[49]</a></sup> After he passed his BA exams in early 1943, Mandela returned to Johannesburg to follow a political path as a lawyer rather than become a privy councillor in Thembuland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994122,_126–27Smith201049Meredith201034Sampson201134_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994122,_126–27Smith201049Meredith201034Sampson201134-51">[50]</a></sup> He later stated that he experienced no epiphany, but that he "simply found [himself] doing so, and could not do otherwise."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994135_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994135-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_activity">Revolutionary activity</span></h3>
<h4><span id="Law_studies_and_the_ANC_Youth_League:_1943.E2.80.931949"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_studies_and_the_ANC_Youth_League:_1943–1949">Law studies and the ANC Youth League: 1943–1949</span></h4>
<p>Mandela began studying law at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Witwatersrand" title="University of the Witwatersrand">University of the Witwatersrand</a>, where he was the only black African student and faced racism. There, he befriended liberal and communist European, Jewish and Indian students, among them <a href="/wiki/Joe_Slovo" title="Joe Slovo">Joe Slovo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruth_First" title="Ruth First">Ruth First</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198833–34Mandela1994127–131Smith201064–65Meredith201034–35Sampson201134–35_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198833–34Mandela1994127–131Smith201064–65Meredith201034–35Sampson201134–35-53">[52]</a></sup> Becoming increasingly politicised, Mandela marched in August 1943 in support of a successful bus boycott to reverse fare rises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994122–123Lodge200627–28Smith201048Meredith201044Sampson201137_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994122–123Lodge200627–28Smith201048Meredith201044Sampson201137-54">[53]</a></sup> Joining the ANC, he was increasingly influenced by Sisulu, spending time with other activists at Sisulu's <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Soweto" title="Orlando, Soweto">Orlando</a> house, including his old friend Oliver Tambo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994136Smith201053Meredith201036,_43_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994136Smith201053Meredith201036,_43-55">[54]</a></sup> In 1943, Mandela met <a href="/wiki/Anton_Lembede" title="Anton Lembede">Anton Lembede</a>, an ANC member affiliated with the "Africanist" branch of <a href="/wiki/African_nationalism" title="African nationalism">African nationalism</a>, which was virulently opposed to a racially united front against colonialism and imperialism or to an alliance with the communists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994137–139Lodge200633–34Smith201053Meredith201042–43Sampson201138–39_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994137–139Lodge200633–34Smith201053Meredith201042–43Sampson201138–39-56">[55]</a></sup> Despite his friendships with non-blacks and communists, Mandela embraced Lembede's views, believing that black Africans should be entirely independent in their struggle for political self-determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198631Meer198834–35Mandela1994142–143Smith201054_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198631Meer198834–35Mandela1994142–143Smith201054-57">[56]</a></sup> Deciding on the need for a youth wing to mass-mobilise Africans in opposition to their subjugation, Mandela was among a delegation that approached ANC president <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Bitini_Xuma" title="Alfred Bitini Xuma">Alfred Bitini Xuma</a> on the subject at his home in <a href="/wiki/Sophiatown" title="Sophiatown">Sophiatown</a>; the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress_Youth_League" title="African National Congress Youth League">African National Congress Youth League</a> (ANCYL) was founded on Easter Sunday 1944 in the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_Men%27s_Social_Centre" title="Bantu Men's Social Centre">Bantu Men's Social Centre</a>, with Lembede as president and Mandela as a member of its executive committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198628–29Mandela1994139–143Lodge200635Smith201052–56Meredith201044–46Sampson201139–41_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198628–29Mandela1994139–143Lodge200635Smith201052–56Meredith201044–46Sampson201139–41-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="392" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg/220px-Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg/330px-Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_e_Evelyn_1944.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela and Evelyn in July 1944, at Walter and Albertina Sisulu's wedding party in the Bantu Men's Social Centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2010inset_photographs_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2010inset_photographs-59">[58]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>At Sisulu's house, Mandela met <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Mase" title="Evelyn Mase">Evelyn Mase</a>, a trainee nurse and ANC activist from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engcobo" title="Engcobo">Engcobo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkei</a>. Entering a relationship and marrying in October 1944, they initially lived with her relatives until moving into a rented house in the township of <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Soweto" title="Orlando, Soweto">Orlando</a> in early 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198624Meer198839–40Mandela1994144,_148–149Lodge200624,_25Smith201059–62Meredith201047Sampson201136_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198624Meer198839–40Mandela1994144,_148–149Lodge200624,_25Smith201059–62Meredith201047Sampson201136-60">[59]</a></sup> Their first child, Madiba "Thembi" Thembekile, was born in February 1945; a daughter, Makaziwe, was born in 1947 but died of <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a> nine months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198840–41Mandela1994149,_152Lodge200629Smith201060–64Meredith201048Sampson201136_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198840–41Mandela1994149,_152Lodge200629Smith201060–64Meredith201048Sampson201136-61">[60]</a></sup> Mandela enjoyed home life, welcoming his mother and his sister, Leabie, to stay with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198840Mandela1994150,_210Lodge200630Smith201067Meredith201048Sampson201136_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198840Mandela1994150,_210Lodge200630Smith201067Meredith201048Sampson201136-62">[61]</a></sup> In early 1947, his three years of articles ended at Witkin, Sidelsky and Eidelman, and he decided to become a full-time student, subsisting on loans from the Bantu Welfare Trust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994151Smith201064Meredith201048–49_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994151Smith201064Meredith201048–49-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1947, Mandela rushed Lembede, who was ill, to hospital, where he died; he was succeeded as ANCYL president by the more moderate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.P._Mda" title="A.P. Mda">Peter Mda</a>, who agreed to co-operate with communists and non-blacks, appointing Mandela ANCYL secretary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198636Meer198843Mandela1994153–154Smith201066Sampson201148_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198636Meer198843Mandela1994153–154Smith201066Sampson201148-64">[63]</a></sup> Mandela disagreed with Mda's approach, and in December 1947 supported an unsuccessful measure to expel communists from the ANCYL, considering their ideology un-African.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994154Sampson201142_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994154Sampson201142-65">[64]</a></sup> In 1947, Mandela was elected to the executive committee of the ANC's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal Province</a> branch, serving under regional president C. S. Ramohanoe. When Ramohanoe acted against the wishes of the committee by co-operating with Indians and communists, Mandela was one of those who forced his resignation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994154–157Lodge200637Smith201066Sampson201149_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994154–157Lodge200637Smith201066Sampson201149-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/1948_South_African_general_election" title="1948 South African general election">South African general election in 1948</a>, in which only whites were permitted to vote, the Afrikaner-dominated <a href="/wiki/Herenigde_Nasionale_Party" title="Herenigde Nasionale Party">Herenigde Nasionale Party</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_Fran%C3%A7ois_Malan" title="Daniel François Malan">Daniel François Malan</a> took power, soon uniting with the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner_Party" title="Afrikaner Party">Afrikaner Party</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a>. Openly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racialism_(Racial_categorization)" title="Racialism (Racial categorization)">racialist</a>, the party codified and expanded racial segregation with new <a href="/wiki/Apartheid_legislation" title="Apartheid legislation">apartheid legislation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198635Mandela1994159–162Lodge200641–42Smith201070–72Meredith201076–78Sampson201151–52_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198635Mandela1994159–162Lodge200641–42Smith201070–72Meredith201076–78Sampson201151–52-67">[66]</a></sup> Gaining increasing influence in the ANC, Mandela and his party cadre allies began advocating <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a> against apartheid, such as boycotts and strikes, influenced by the tactics already employed by South Africa's Indian community. Xuma did not support these measures and was removed from the presidency in a <a href="/wiki/Motion_of_no_confidence" title="Motion of no confidence">vote of no confidence</a>, replaced by <a href="/wiki/James_Moroka" title="James Moroka">James Moroka</a> and a more militant executive committee containing Sisulu, Mda, Tambo and Godfrey Pitje.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198636–37Mandela1994162–165Lodge200644Smith201072–73Meredith201078–79Sampson201153–55_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198636–37Mandela1994162–165Lodge200644Smith201072–73Meredith201078–79Sampson201153–55-68">[67]</a></sup> Mandela later related that he and his colleagues had "guided the ANC to a more radical and revolutionary path."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994165_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994165-69">[68]</a></sup> Having devoted his time to politics, Mandela failed his final year at Witwatersrand three times; he was ultimately denied his degree in December 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith201068–70Sampson201135_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith201068–70Sampson201135-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Defiance_Campaign_and_Transvaal_ANC_Presidency:_1950.E2.80.931954"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Defiance_Campaign_and_Transvaal_ANC_Presidency:_1950–1954">Defiance Campaign and Transvaal ANC Presidency: 1950–1954</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_African_National_Congress.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ANC's tricolour flag; black for the people, green for the land, and gold for the resources of Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198626_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198626-71">[70]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Mandela took Xuma's place on the ANC national executive in March 1950,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994168Lodge200644Sampson201155–56_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994168Lodge200644Sampson201155–56-72">[71]</a></sup> and that same year was elected national president of the ANCYL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198641Mandela1994176Lodge200647Smith201078Meredith201088Sampson201163–64_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198641Mandela1994176Lodge200647Smith201078Meredith201088Sampson201163–64-73">[72]</a></sup> In March, the Defend Free Speech Convention was held in Johannesburg, bringing together African, Indian and communist activists to call a <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day" title="International Workers' Day">May Day</a> <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a> in protest against apartheid and white minority rule. Mandela opposed the strike because it was multi-racial and not ANC-led, but a majority of black workers took part, resulting in increased police repression and the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Suppression_of_Communism_Act,_1950" title="Suppression of Communism Act, 1950">Suppression of Communism Act, 1950</a>, affecting the actions of all protest groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198638–40Meer198848–49Mandela1994165–167Smith201074–75Meredith201081–83Sampson201161–62_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198638–40Meer198848–49Mandela1994165–167Smith201074–75Meredith201081–83Sampson201161–62-74">[73]</a></sup> At the ANC national conference of December 1951, he continued arguing against a racially united front, but was outvoted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994176Smith201078Sampson201163–64_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994176Smith201078Sampson201163–64-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thereafter, Mandela rejected Lembede's Africanism and embraced the idea of a multi-racial front against apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198642Meer198855Lodge200648Meredith201094_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198642Meer198855Lodge200648Meredith201094-76">[75]</a></sup> Influenced by friends like <a href="/wiki/Moses_Kotane" title="Moses Kotane">Moses Kotane</a> and by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>'s support for <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_national_liberation" title="Wars of national liberation">wars of national liberation</a>, his mistrust of communism broke down and he began reading literature by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>, eventually embracing the <a href="/wiki/Marxist_philosophy" title="Marxist philosophy">Marxist philosophy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_materialism" title="Dialectical materialism">dialectical materialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994177–172Lodge200645,_47Smith201075–76Meredith201087Sampson201164–65_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994177–172Lodge200645,_47Smith201075–76Meredith201087Sampson201164–65-77">[76]</a></sup> Commenting on communism, he later stated that he "found [himself] strongly drawn to the idea of a classless society which, to [his] mind, was similar to traditional African culture where life was shared and communal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994172_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994172-78">[77]</a></sup> In April 1952, Mandela began work at the H.M. Basner law firm, which was owned by a communist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994165Lodge200653Smith201077Meredith201092_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994165Lodge200653Smith201077Meredith201092-79">[78]</a></sup> although his increasing commitment to work and activism meant he spent less time with his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994170Smith201094Meredith2010103_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994170Smith201094Meredith2010103-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1952, the ANC began preparation for a joint <a href="/wiki/Defiance_Campaign" title="Defiance Campaign">Defiance Campaign</a> against apartheid with Indian and communist groups, founding a National Voluntary Board to recruit volunteers. The campaign was designed to follow the path of <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_resistance" title="Nonviolent resistance">nonviolent resistance</a> influenced by <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a>; some supported this for ethical reasons, but Mandela instead considered it pragmatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198644–46Meer198856–58Mandela1994182–183Smith201077,_80Meredith201088–89Sampson201166–67_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198644–46Meer198856–58Mandela1994182–183Smith201077,_80Meredith201088–89Sampson201166–67-81">[80]</a></sup> At a <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a> rally on 22 June, Mandela addressed an assembled crowd of 10,000 people, initiating the campaign protests for which he was arrested and briefly interned in Marshall Square prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994183–188Lodge200652,_53_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994183–188Lodge200652,_53-82">[81]</a></sup> These events established Mandela as one of the best-known black political figures in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge200647_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge200647-83">[82]</a></sup> With further protests, the ANC's membership grew from 20,000 to 100,000 members; the government responded with mass arrests and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Public_Safety_Act,_1953" title="Public Safety Act, 1953">Public Safety Act, 1953</a> to permit <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994188–192Sampson201168_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994188–192Sampson201168-84">[83]</a></sup> In May, authorities banned Transvaal ANC president <a href="/wiki/J._B._Marks" title="J. B. Marks">J. B. Marks</a> from making public appearances; unable to maintain his position, he recommended Mandela as his successor. Although Africanists opposed his candidacy, Mandela was elected to be regional president in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198651Mandela1994194–195Lodge200654Smith201085Sampson201172–73_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198651Mandela1994194–195Lodge200654Smith201085Sampson201172–73-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1045" data-file-width="1462" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg/220px-The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg/330px-The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg/440px-The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Nelson_Mandela_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mandela_House" title="Mandela House">Mandela's former home</a> in the Johannesburg township of <a href="/wiki/Soweto" title="Soweto">Soweto</a></div></div></div>
<p>In July 1952, Mandela was arrested under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suppression_of_Communism_Act" title="Suppression of Communism Act">Suppression of Communism Act</a> and stood trial as one of the 21 accused—among them Moroka, Sisulu and <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_Dadoo" title="Yusuf Dadoo">Yusuf Dadoo</a>—in Johannesburg. Found guilty of "statutory communism", a term that the government used to describe most opposition to apartheid, their sentence of nine months' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_labour" title="Hard labour">hard labour</a> was <a href="/wiki/Suspended_sentence" title="Suspended sentence">suspended</a> for two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198650–51Mandela1994195–198Lodge200654Smith201083–84Meredith201092Sampson201171–72_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198650–51Mandela1994195–198Lodge200654Smith201083–84Meredith201092Sampson201171–72-86">[85]</a></sup> In December, Mandela was given a six-month ban from attending meetings or talking to more than one individual at a time, making his Transvaal ANC presidency impractical, and during this period the Defiance Campaign petered out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198864Mandela1994199–200,_204Smith201086Sampson201173_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198864Mandela1994199–200,_204Smith201086Sampson201173-87">[86]</a></sup> In September 1953, Andrew Kunene read out Mandela's "No Easy Walk to Freedom" speech at a Transvaal ANC meeting; the title was taken from a quote by Indian independence leader <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>, a seminal influence on Mandela's thought. The speech laid out a contingency plan for a scenario in which the ANC was banned. This Mandela Plan, or M-Plan, involved dividing the organisation into a <a href="/wiki/Clandestine_cell_system" title="Clandestine cell system">cell structure</a> with a more centralised leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198658–59Meer198860Mandela1994205–207,_231Lodge200658Meredith2010107–108Smith2010116–117Sampson201181–82,_84–85_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198658–59Meer198860Mandela1994205–207,_231Lodge200658Meredith2010107–108Smith2010116–117Sampson201181–82,_84–85-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela obtained work as an attorney for the firm Terblanche and Briggish, before moving to the liberal-run Helman and Michel, passing qualification exams to become a full-fledged attorney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994209–210Smith201087Meredith201095Sampson20117_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994209–210Smith201087Meredith201095Sampson20117-89">[88]</a></sup> In August 1953, Mandela and Tambo opened their own law firm, <a href="/wiki/Mandela_and_Tambo" title="Mandela and Tambo">Mandela and Tambo</a>, operating in downtown Johannesburg. The only African-run law firm in the country, it was popular with aggrieved black people, often dealing with cases of <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a>. Disliked by the authorities, the firm was forced to relocate to a remote location after their office permit was removed under the <a href="/wiki/Group_Areas_Act" title="Group Areas Act">Group Areas Act</a>; as a result, their clientele dwindled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198654–57Meer198861Mandela1994210–216Lodge200673Smith201087–93Meredith201095–101Sampson201177–80_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198654–57Meer198861Mandela1994210–216Lodge200673Smith201087–93Meredith201095–101Sampson201177–80-90">[89]</a></sup> As a lawyer of aristocratic heritage, Mandela was part of Johannesburg's elite black middle-class, and accorded much respect from the black community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge200628–29,_75_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge200628–29,_75-91">[90]</a></sup> Although a second daughter, <a href="/wiki/Makaziwe_Mandela" title="Makaziwe Mandela">Makaziwe Phumia</a>, was born in May 1954, Mandela's relationship with Evelyn became strained, and she accused him of adultery. He may have had affairs with ANC member <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Ngoyi" title="Lillian Ngoyi">Lillian Ngoyi</a> and secretary Ruth Mompati; various individuals close to Mandela in this period have stated that the latter bore him a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010103–104Smith201095–99,_105–106_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010103–104Smith201095–99,_105–106-92">[91]</a></sup> Disgusted by her son's behaviour, Nosekeni returned to Transkei, while Evelyn embraced the <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> and rejected Mandela's preoccupation with politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994293–294Meredith2010104–105Smith201098–99,_105–106Sampson201176–77_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994293–294Meredith2010104–105Smith201098–99,_105–106Sampson201176–77-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Congress_of_the_People_and_the_Treason_Trial:_1955.E2.80.931961"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Congress_of_the_People_and_the_Treason_Trial:_1955–1961">Congress of the People and the Treason Trial: 1955–1961</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treason_Trial" title="Treason Trial">Treason Trial</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>We, the people of South Africa, declare for all our country and the world to know:<br/>That South Africa belongs to all who live in it, black and white, and that no government can justly claim authority unless it is based on the will of the people.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Opening words of the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Charter" title="Freedom Charter">Freedom Charter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198666Sampson201192_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198666Sampson201192-94">[93]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>After taking part in the unsuccessful protest to prevent the forced relocation of all black people from the Sophiatown suburb of Johannesburg in February 1955, Mandela concluded that violent action would prove necessary to end apartheid and white minority rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994218–233,_234–236Lodge200659–60Meredith2010114–117Smith2010120–123Sampson201182–84_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994218–233,_234–236Lodge200659–60Meredith2010114–117Smith2010120–123Sampson201182–84-95">[94]</a></sup> On his advice, Sisulu requested weaponry from the People's Republic of China, which was denied. Although the Chinese government supported the anti-apartheid struggle, they believed the movement insufficiently prepared for <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994226–227Lodge200660Meredith2010108–109Smith2010118Sampson201184_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994226–227Lodge200660Meredith2010108–109Smith2010118Sampson201184-96">[95]</a></sup> With the involvement of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Indian_Congress" title="South African Indian Congress">South African Indian Congress</a>, the Coloured People's Congress, the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Congress_of_Trade_Unions" title="South African Congress of Trade Unions">South African Congress of Trade Unions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Congress_of_Democrats" title="South African Congress of Democrats">Congress of Democrats</a>, the ANC planned a <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_People_(1955)" title="Congress of the People (1955)">Congress of the People</a>, calling on all South Africans to send in proposals for a post-apartheid era. Based on the responses, a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Charter" title="Freedom Charter">Freedom Charter</a> was drafted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rusty_Bernstein" title="Rusty Bernstein">Rusty Bernstein</a>, calling for the creation of a democratic, non-racialist state with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalisation" title="Nationalisation">nationalisation</a> of major industry. The charter was adopted at a June 1955 conference in <a href="/wiki/Kliptown" title="Kliptown">Kliptown</a>; 3,000 delegates attended the event, which was forcibly closed down by police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198664–67Meer198871–75Mandela1994243–249Lodge200665–66Meredith2010129–133Smith2010118–120,_125–128Sampson201187–95_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198664–67Meer198871–75Mandela1994243–249Lodge200665–66Meredith2010129–133Smith2010118–120,_125–128Sampson201187–95-97">[96]</a></sup> The tenets of the Freedom Charter remained important for Mandela, and in 1956 he described it as "an inspiration to the people of South Africa".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010134_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010134-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the end of a second ban in September 1955, Mandela went on a working holiday to <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkei</a> to discuss the implications of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_Authorities_Act,_1951" title="Bantu Authorities Act, 1951">Bantu Authorities Act, 1951</a> with local Xhosa chiefs, also visiting his mother and Noengland before proceeding to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994253–274Smith2010130–132Sampson201196–99_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994253–274Smith2010130–132Sampson201196–99-99">[98]</a></sup> In March 1956, he received his third ban on public appearances, restricting him to Johannesburg for five years, but he often defied it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994275Meredith2010147Sampson2011101–102_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994275Meredith2010147Sampson2011101–102-100">[99]</a></sup> Mandela's marriage broke down and Evelyn left him, taking their children to live with her brother. Initiating divorce proceedings in May 1956, she claimed that Mandela had physically abused her; he denied the allegations, and fought for custody of their children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198879–80Meredith2010143–144Smith2010100–102Sampson2011110_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198879–80Meredith2010143–144Smith2010100–102Sampson2011110-101">[100]</a></sup> She withdrew her petition of separation in November, but Mandela filed for divorce in January 1958; the divorce was finalised in March, with the children placed in Evelyn's care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198879–80Mandela1994296Smith2010102–104Sampson2011110_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198879–80Mandela1994296Smith2010102–104Sampson2011110-102">[101]</a></sup> During the divorce proceedings, he began courting a social worker, <a href="/wiki/Winnie_Madikizela-Mandela" title="Winnie Madikizela-Mandela">Winnie Madikizela</a>, whom he married in <a href="/wiki/Bizana,_Eastern_Cape" title="Bizana, Eastern Cape">Bizana</a> in June 1958. She later became involved in ANC activities, spending several weeks in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198674–76Meer198893Mandela1994306–311Lodge200675–77Meredith2010144–149Smith2010104,_132–145Sampson2011110–113_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198674–76Meer198893Mandela1994306–311Lodge200675–77Meredith2010144–149Smith2010104,_132–145Sampson2011110–113-103">[102]</a></sup> Together they had two children: <a href="/wiki/Zenani_Mandela-Dlamini" title="Zenani Mandela-Dlamini">Zenani</a>, born in February 1959, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zindzi_Mandela-Hlongwane" title="Zindzi Mandela-Hlongwane">Zindziswa</a> (1960–2020).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010165,_186_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010165,_186-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="665" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/220px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/330px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/440px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An apartheid sign; apartheid legislation impacted all areas of life</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1956, Mandela was arrested alongside most of the ANC national executive, and accused of "high treason" against the state. Held in Johannesburg Prison amid mass protests, they underwent a preparatory examination before being granted bail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198668,_71–72Meer198883Mandela1994283–292Meredith2010136–141Smith2010163–164Sampson2011103–106_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198668,_71–72Meer198883Mandela1994283–292Meredith2010136–141Smith2010163–164Sampson2011103–106-105">[104]</a></sup> The defence's refutation began in January 1957, overseen by defence lawyer <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Berrang%C3%A9" title="Vernon Berrangé">Vernon Berrangé</a>, and continued until the case was adjourned in September. In January 1958, <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Pirow" title="Oswald Pirow">Oswald Pirow</a> was appointed to prosecute the case, and in February the judge ruled that there was "sufficient reason" for the defendants to go on trial in the <a href="/wiki/Gauteng_Division" title="Gauteng Division">Transvaal Supreme Court</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994299–305Meredith2010142Smith2010167–168Sampson2011116–117_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994299–305Meredith2010142Smith2010167–168Sampson2011116–117-106">[105]</a></sup> The formal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treason_Trial" title="Treason Trial">Treason Trial</a> began in <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a> in August 1958, with the defendants successfully applying to have the three judges—all linked to the governing National Party—replaced. In August, one charge was dropped, and in October the prosecution withdrew its indictment, submitting a reformulated version in November which argued that the ANC leadership committed high treason by advocating violent revolution, a charge the defendants denied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994331–334Meredith2010162,_165Smith2010167Sampson2011122–123_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994331–334Meredith2010162,_165Smith2010167Sampson2011122–123-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1959, Africanists dissatisfied with the ANC's united front approach founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan-Africanist_Congress" title="Pan-Africanist Congress">Pan-Africanist Congress</a> (PAC); Mandela disagreed with the PAC's racially exclusionary views, describing them as "immature" and "naïve".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198679Meer198890–92,_141–143Mandela1994327–330Meredith2010167–168Smith2010171–173Sampson2011117–122_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198679Meer198890–92,_141–143Mandela1994327–330Meredith2010167–168Smith2010171–173Sampson2011117–122-108">[107]</a></sup> Both parties took part in an anti-pass campaign in early 1960, in which Africans burned <a href="/wiki/Pass_laws" title="Pass laws">the passes that they were legally obliged to carry</a>. One of the PAC-organised demonstrations was fired upon by police, resulting in the deaths of 69 protesters in the <a href="/wiki/Sharpeville_massacre" title="Sharpeville massacre">Sharpeville massacre</a>. The incident brought international condemnation of the government and resulted in rioting throughout South Africa, with Mandela publicly burning his pass in solidarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198683–84Meer1988144–147Mandela1994342–346Lodge200681–82Meredith2010167–170Smith2010173–175Sampson2011130–131_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198683–84Meer1988144–147Mandela1994342–346Lodge200681–82Meredith2010167–170Smith2010173–175Sampson2011130–131-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Responding to the unrest, the government implemented state of emergency measures, declaring <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> and banning the ANC and PAC; in March, they arrested Mandela and other activists, imprisoning them for five months without charge in the unsanitary conditions of the Pretoria Local prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198685–86Mandela1994347–357Meredith2010172–175Smith2010175Sampson2011132–133_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198685–86Mandela1994347–357Meredith2010172–175Smith2010175Sampson2011132–133-110">[109]</a></sup> Imprisonment caused problems for Mandela and his co-defendants in the Treason Trial; their lawyers could not reach them, and so it was decided that the lawyers would withdraw in protest until the accused were freed from prison when the state of emergency was lifted in late August 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994357–364Meredith2010176,_184Smith2010177Sampson2011134–135_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994357–364Meredith2010176,_184Smith2010177Sampson2011134–135-111">[110]</a></sup> Over the following months, Mandela used his free time to organise an All-In African Conference near <a href="/wiki/Pietermaritzburg" title="Pietermaritzburg">Pietermaritzburg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natal_Province" title="Natal Province">Natal</a>, in March 1961, at which 1,400 anti-apartheid delegates met, agreeing on a stay-at-home strike to mark 31 May, the day South Africa became a republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198698Mandela1994373–374Lodge200683–84Meredith2010187–188Smith2010183–185Sampson2011140–143_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198698Mandela1994373–374Lodge200683–84Meredith2010187–188Smith2010183–185Sampson2011140–143-112">[111]</a></sup> On 29 March 1961, six years after the Treason Trial began, the judges produced a verdict of not guilty, ruling that there was insufficient evidence to convict the accused of "high treason", since they had advocated neither communism nor violent revolution; the outcome embarrassed the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198694Meer1988151Mandela1994377–380Lodge200684Meredith2010188–189Smith2010178Sampson2011143_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198694Meer1988151Mandela1994377–380Lodge200684Meredith2010188–189Smith2010178Sampson2011143-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="MK.2C_the_SACP.2C_and_African_tour:_1961.E2.80.9362"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="MK,_the_SACP,_and_African_tour:_1961–62">MK, the SACP, and African tour: 1961–62</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg/220px-Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg/330px-Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg/440px-Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liliesleaf_hut1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatched room at Liliesleaf Farm, where Mandela hid</div></div></div>
<p>Disguised as a chauffeur, Mandela travelled around the country incognito, organising the ANC's new cell structure and the planned mass stay-at-home strike. Referred to as the "Black Pimpernel" in the press—a reference to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emma_Orczy" title="Emma Orczy">Emma Orczy</a>'s 1905 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scarlet_Pimpernel" title="The Scarlet Pimpernel">The Scarlet Pimpernel</a></i>—a warrant for his arrest was put out by the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198699Mandela1994283–287Meredith2010192–193Smith2010186–188,_193Sampson2011144–146,_154_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198699Mandela1994283–287Meredith2010192–193Smith2010186–188,_193Sampson2011144–146,_154-114">[113]</a></sup> Mandela held secret meetings with reporters, and after the government failed to prevent the strike, he warned them that many anti-apartheid activists would soon resort to violence through groups like the PAC's <a href="/wiki/Azanian_People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="Azanian People's Liberation Army">Poqo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994289–291Smith2010188–189Sampson2011147–149_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994289–291Smith2010188–189Sampson2011147–149-115">[114]</a></sup> He believed that the ANC should form an armed group to channel some of this violence in a controlled direction, convincing both ANC leader <a href="/wiki/Albert_Luthuli" title="Albert Luthuli">Albert Luthuli</a>—who was morally opposed to violence—and allied activist groups of its necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994393–396Meredith2010199–200Smith2010206–210Sampson2011150–151_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994393–396Meredith2010199–200Smith2010206–210Sampson2011150–151-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inspired by the actions of <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>'s <a href="/wiki/26th_of_July_Movement" title="26th of July Movement">26th of July Movement</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a>, in 1961 Mandela, Sisulu and Slovo co-founded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umkhonto_we_Sizwe" title="Umkhonto we Sizwe">Umkhonto we Sizwe</a> ("Spear of the Nation", abbreviated MK). Becoming chairman of the militant group, Mandela gained ideas from literature on guerrilla warfare by Marxist militants Mao and <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a> as well as from the military theorist <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986107Mandela1994397–398Meredith2010197–198,_200–201Smith2010209–214Sampson2011151–154_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986107Mandela1994397–398Meredith2010197–198,_200–201Smith2010209–214Sampson2011151–154-117">[116]</a></sup> Although initially declared officially separate from the ANC so as not to taint the latter's reputation, MK was later widely recognised as the party's armed wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2010209–210Sampson2011151_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2010209–210Sampson2011151-118">[117]</a></sup> Most early MK members were white communists who were able to conceal Mandela in their homes; after hiding in communist Wolfie Kodesh's flat in <a href="/wiki/Berea,_Gauteng" title="Berea, Gauteng">Berea</a>, Mandela moved to the communist-owned <a href="/wiki/Liliesleaf_Farm" title="Liliesleaf Farm">Liliesleaf Farm</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rivonia" title="Rivonia">Rivonia</a>, there joined by <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Mhlaba" title="Raymond Mhlaba">Raymond Mhlaba</a>, Slovo and Bernstein, who put together the MK constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986107Mandela1994397–409Lodge200692–93Meredith2010201–204Smith2010191,_222–229Sampson2011154–156_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986107Mandela1994397–409Lodge200692–93Meredith2010201–204Smith2010191,_222–229Sampson2011154–156-119">[118]</a></sup> Although in later life Mandela denied, for political reasons, ever being a member of the Communist Party, historical research published in 2011 strongly suggested that he had joined in the late 1950s or early 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2011667–668_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2011667–668-120">[119]</a></sup> This was confirmed by both the SACP and the ANC after Mandela's death. According to the SACP, he was not only a member of the party, but also served on its Central Committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis20161_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis20161-121">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandela'sCommunism_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela'sCommunism-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em; ;">
