<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>We of Umkhonto have always sought to achieve liberation without bloodshed and civil clash. Even at this late hour, we hope that our first actions will awaken everyone to a realization of the dangerous situation to which Nationalist policy is leading. We hope that we will bring the Government and its supporters to their senses before it is too late so that both government and its policies can be changed before matters reach the desperate stage of civil war.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Statement released by MK to announce the start of their sabotage campaign<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988171Meredith2010207_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988171Meredith2010207-123">[122]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Operating through a cell structure, MK planned to carry out acts of sabotage that would exert maximum pressure on the government with minimum casualties; they sought to bomb military installations, power plants, telephone lines, and transport links at night, when civilians were not present. Mandela stated that they chose sabotage because it was the least harmful action, did not involve killing, and offered the best hope for racial reconciliation afterwards; he nevertheless acknowledged that should this have failed then guerrilla warfare might have been necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986108Meer1988171Mandela1994411–412Lodge200690Meredith2010204_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986108Meer1988171Mandela1994411–412Lodge200690Meredith2010204-124">[123]</a></sup> Soon after ANC leader Luthuli was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>, MK publicly announced its existence with 57 bombings on <a href="/wiki/Day_of_the_Vow" title="Day of the Vow">Dingane's Day</a> (16 December) 1961, followed by further attacks on New Year's Eve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986110Meer1988170Mandela1994413–415Lodge200695Meredith2010206Smith2010239–246Sampson2011158–159_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986110Meer1988170Mandela1994413–415Lodge200695Meredith2010206Smith2010239–246Sampson2011158–159-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ANC decided to send Mandela as a delegate to the February 1962 meeting of the Pan-African Freedom Movement for East, Central and Southern Africa (PAFMECSA) in <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986111Meer1988171–172,_176Mandela1994418–425Lodge200695Smith2010251–254Benneyworth201181Sampson2011160–162_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986111Meer1988171–172,_176Mandela1994418–425Lodge200695Smith2010251–254Benneyworth201181Sampson2011160–162-126">[125]</a></sup> Leaving South Africa in secret via <a href="/wiki/Bechuanaland_Protectorate" title="Bechuanaland Protectorate">Bechuanaland</a>, on his way Mandela visited <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(1961%E2%80%931964)" title="Tanganyika (1961–1964)">Tanganyika</a> and met with its president, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988173–175Lodge200697Meredith2010209Benneyworth201181,_84_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988173–175Lodge200697Meredith2010209Benneyworth201181,_84-127">[126]</a></sup> Arriving in Ethiopia, Mandela met with Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie_I" title="Haile Selassie I">Haile Selassie I</a>, and gave his speech after Selassie's at the conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988176–177,_180Mandela1994427–432Smith2010255–256Sampson2011163–165_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988176–177,_180Mandela1994427–432Smith2010255–256Sampson2011163–165-128">[127]</a></sup> After the symposium, he travelled to <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, Egypt, admiring the political reforms of President <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, and then went to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>, Tunisia, where President <a href="/wiki/Habib_Bourguiba" title="Habib Bourguiba">Habib Bourguiba</a> gave him £5,000 for weaponry. He proceeded to Morocco, Mali, Guinea, Sierra Leone, Liberia and Senegal, receiving funds from Liberian president <a href="/wiki/William_Tubman" title="William Tubman">William Tubman</a> and Guinean president <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9" title="Ahmed Sékou Touré">Ahmed Sékou Touré</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988185–194Mandela1994432–440Meredith2010210Smith2010256–259Sampson2011165–167_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988185–194Mandela1994432–440Meredith2010210Smith2010256–259Sampson2011165–167-129">[128]</a></sup> He left Africa for London, England, where he met anti-apartheid activists, reporters and prominent politicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986114Meer1988196–197Mandela1994441–443Meredith2010210–211Smith2010259–261Sampson2011167–169_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986114Meer1988196–197Mandela1994441–443Meredith2010210–211Smith2010259–261Sampson2011167–169-130">[129]</a></sup> Upon returning to Ethiopia, he began a six-month course in guerrilla warfare, but completed only two months before being recalled to South Africa by the ANC's leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994443–445Lodge2006100Meredith2010211Smith2010261–262Benneyworth201191–93Sampson2011169–170_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994443–445Lodge2006100Meredith2010211Smith2010261–262Benneyworth201191–93Sampson2011169–170-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imprisonment">Imprisonment</span></h3>
<h4><span id="Arrest_and_Rivonia_trial:_1962.E2.80.931964"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_and_Rivonia_trial:_1962–1964">Arrest and Rivonia trial: 1962–1964</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rivonia_Trial" title="Rivonia Trial">Rivonia Trial</a></div>
<p>On 5 August 1962, police captured Mandela along with fellow activist <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Williams_(anti-apartheid_activist)" title="Cecil Williams (anti-apartheid activist)">Cecil Williams</a> near <a href="/wiki/Howick,_KwaZulu-Natal" title="Howick, KwaZulu-Natal">Howick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986116–117Meer1988201–202Mandela1994435–435Meredith2010215–216Smith2010275–276Sampson2011170–172_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986116–117Meer1988201–202Mandela1994435–435Meredith2010215–216Smith2010275–276Sampson2011170–172-132">[131]</a></sup> Many MK members suspected that the authorities had been tipped off with regard to Mandela's whereabouts, although Mandela himself gave these ideas little credence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994278–279Meredith2010216Sampson2011172_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994278–279Meredith2010216Sampson2011172-133">[132]</a></sup> In later years, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rickard" title="Donald Rickard">Donald Rickard</a>, a former American diplomat, revealed that the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a>, which feared Mandela's associations with communists, had informed the South African police of his location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010216–217Sampson2011172_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010216–217Sampson2011172-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> Jailed in Johannesburg's Marshall Square prison, Mandela was charged with inciting workers' strikes and leaving the country without permission. Representing himself with Slovo as legal advisor, Mandela intended to use the trial to showcase "the ANC's moral opposition to racism" while supporters demonstrated outside the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994456–459Sampson2011172–173_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994456–459Sampson2011172–173-136">[135]</a></sup> Moved to <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>, where Winnie could visit him, he began correspondence studies for a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Laws" title="Bachelor of Laws">Bachelor of Laws</a> (LLB) degree from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_London_International_Programmes" title="University of London International Programmes">University of London International Programmes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994463–465Smith2010292–293Sampson2011173–174_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994463–465Smith2010292–293Sampson2011173–174-137">[136]</a></sup> His hearing began in October, but he disrupted proceedings by wearing a traditional <i><a href="/wiki/Kaross" title="Kaross">kaross</a></i>, refusing to call any witnesses, and turning his plea of mitigation into a political speech. Found guilty, he was sentenced to five years' imprisonment; as he left the courtroom, supporters sang "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nkosi_Sikelel_iAfrika" title="Nkosi Sikelel iAfrika">Nkosi Sikelel iAfrika</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986120–134Meer1988210–213Mandela1994468–482Lodge2006104–106Meredith2010218–426Sampson2011174–176_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986120–134Meer1988210–213Mandela1994468–482Lodge2006104–106Meredith2010218–426Sampson2011174–176-138">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>I have fought against white domination, and I have fought against black domination. I have cherished the ideal of a democratic and free society in which all persons will live together in harmony and with equal opportunities. It is an ideal which I hope to live for and to see realised. But if it needs be, it is an ideal for which I am prepared to die.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Mandela's <a href="/wiki/I_Am_Prepared_to_Die" title="I Am Prepared to Die">Rivonia Trial Speech</a>, 1964<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986159Meer1988258Meredith2010265Smith2010302Sampson2011193Broun201274_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986159Meer1988258Meredith2010265Smith2010302Sampson2011193Broun201274-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>On 11 July 1963, police raided <a href="/wiki/Liliesleaf_Farm" title="Liliesleaf Farm">Liliesleaf Farm</a>, arresting those that they found there and uncovering paperwork documenting MK's activities, some of which mentioned Mandela. The <a href="/wiki/Rivonia_Trial" title="Rivonia Trial">Rivonia Trial</a> began at <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_South_Africa" title="Supreme Court of South Africa">Pretoria Supreme Court</a> in October, with Mandela and his comrades charged with four counts of sabotage and conspiracy to violently overthrow the government; their chief prosecutor was <a href="/wiki/Percy_Yutar" title="Percy Yutar">Percy Yutar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986134–137Meer1988223–226Mandela200427–32Lodge2006108–109Meredith2010242–250Smith2010292–295Sampson2011183–186Broun20126–10,_19–20_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986134–137Meer1988223–226Mandela200427–32Lodge2006108–109Meredith2010242–250Smith2010292–295Sampson2011183–186Broun20126–10,_19–20-141">[140]</a></sup> Judge <a href="/wiki/Quartus_de_Wet" title="Quartus de Wet">Quartus de Wet</a> soon threw out the prosecution's case for insufficient evidence, but Yutar reformulated the charges, presenting his new case from December 1963 until February 1964, calling 173 witnesses and bringing thousands of documents and photographs to the trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986138–139Meer1988226Mandela200433–42Meredith2010252–254,_256Sampson2011186–190Broun201243–49_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986138–139Meer1988226Mandela200433–42Meredith2010252–254,_256Sampson2011186–190Broun201243–49-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although four of the accused denied involvement with MK, Mandela and the other five accused admitted sabotage but denied that they had ever agreed to initiate guerrilla war against the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986160Meer1988232–233Mandela200442–44Meredith2010252,_259_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986160Meer1988232–233Mandela200442–44Meredith2010252,_259-143">[142]</a></sup> They used the trial to highlight their political cause; at the opening of the defence's proceedings, Mandela gave his three-hour "<a href="/wiki/I_Am_Prepared_to_Die" title="I Am Prepared to Die">I Am Prepared to Die</a>" speech. That speech—which was inspired by Castro's "<a href="/wiki/History_Will_Absolve_Me" title="History Will Absolve Me">History Will Absolve Me</a>"—was widely reported in the press despite official censorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986140Mandela200443–57Meredith2010258–265Smith2010298–302Sampson2011191–194Broun201268–75_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986140Mandela200443–57Meredith2010258–265Smith2010298–302Sampson2011191–194Broun201268–75-144">[143]</a></sup> The trial gained international attention; there were global calls for the release of the accused from the United Nations and <a href="/wiki/World_Peace_Council" title="World Peace Council">World Peace Council</a>, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_London_Union" title="University of London Union">University of London Union</a> voted Mandela to its presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela200462Meredith2010268Smith2010303Sampson2011194–195Broun2012102–104,_107_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela200462Meredith2010268Smith2010303Sampson2011194–195Broun2012102–104,_107-145">[144]</a></sup> On 12 June 1964, justice De Wet found Mandela and two of his co-accused guilty on all four charges; although the prosecution had called for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_sentence" title="Death sentence">death sentence</a> to be applied, the judge instead condemned them to <a href="/wiki/Life_imprisonment" title="Life imprisonment">life imprisonment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986161,_163Mandela200463–68Meredith2010268–272Smith2010306Sampson2011196–197Broun2012116–128_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986161,_163Mandela200463–68Meredith2010268–272Smith2010306Sampson2011196–197Broun2012116–128-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Robben_Island:_1964.E2.80.931982"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Robben_Island:_1964–1982">Robben Island: 1964–1982</span></h4>
<p>In 1964, Mandela and his co-accused were transferred from Pretoria to the prison on <a href="/wiki/Robben_Island" title="Robben Island">Robben Island</a>, remaining there for the next 18 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986165Meer1988262Mandela200475–78Smith2010307–308Sampson2011204_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986165Meer1988262Mandela200475–78Smith2010307–308Sampson2011204-147">[146]</a></sup> Isolated from non-political prisoners in Section B, Mandela was imprisoned in a damp concrete cell measuring 8 feet (2.4 m) by 7 feet (2.1 m), with a straw mat on which to sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela200479–80Meredith2010279Sampson2011205_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela200479–80Meredith2010279Sampson2011205-148">[147]</a></sup> Verbally and physically harassed by several white prison wardens, the Rivonia Trial prisoners spent their days breaking rocks into gravel, until being reassigned in January 1965 to work in a lime quarry. Mandela was initially forbidden to wear sunglasses, and the glare from the lime permanently damaged his eyesight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986166,_182Meer1988266Mandela200482–84,_108–116Meredith2010281–283,_290–291Sampson2011206–207_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986166,_182Meer1988266Mandela200482–84,_108–116Meredith2010281–283,_290–291Sampson2011206–207-149">[148]</a></sup> At night, he worked on his LLB degree, which he was obtaining from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_London" title="University of London">University of London</a> through a correspondence course with <a href="/wiki/Wolsey_Hall,_Oxford" title="Wolsey Hall, Oxford">Wolsey Hall, Oxford</a>, but newspapers were forbidden, and he was locked in <a href="/wiki/Solitary_confinement" title="Solitary confinement">solitary confinement</a> on several occasions for the possession of smuggled news clippings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986174Mandela2004126Meredith2010299Sampson2011205,_258_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986174Mandela2004126Meredith2010299Sampson2011205,_258-150">[149]</a></sup> He was initially classified as the lowest grade of prisoner, Class D, meaning that he was permitted one visit and one letter every six months, although all mail was heavily censored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986169Mandela2004102–108Meredith2010283Sampson2011205_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986169Mandela2004102–108Meredith2010283Sampson2011205-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG/220px-RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG/330px-RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG/440px-RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RobbenIslandSteinbruchA.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lime quarry on Robben Island where Mandela and other prisoners were forced to carry out hard labour</div></div></div>
<p>The political prisoners took part in work and <a href="/wiki/Hunger_strike" title="Hunger strike">hunger strikes</a>—the latter considered largely ineffective by Mandela—to improve prison conditions, viewing this as a microcosm of the anti-apartheid struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986175Mandela200483,_90,_136–138Lodge2006124Meredith2010284,_296–298_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986175Mandela200483,_90,_136–138Lodge2006124Meredith2010284,_296–298-152">[151]</a></sup> ANC prisoners elected him to their four-man "High Organ" along with Sisulu, <a href="/wiki/Govan_Mbeki" title="Govan Mbeki">Govan Mbeki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Mhlaba" title="Raymond Mhlaba">Raymond Mhlaba</a>, and he involved himself in a group, named Ulundi, that represented all political prisoners (including <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Daniels_(political_activist)" title="Eddie Daniels (political activist)">Eddie Daniels</a>) on the island, through which he forged links with PAC and <a href="/wiki/Yu_Chi_Chan_Club" title="Yu Chi Chan Club">Yu Chi Chan Club</a> members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010298–299Sampson2011210–214_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010298–299Sampson2011210–214-153">[152]</a></sup> Initiating the "University of Robben Island", whereby prisoners lectured on their own areas of expertise, he debated socio-political topics with his comrades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006130–131Meredith2010292–295Sampson2011236–241,_288–294_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006130–131Meredith2010292–295Sampson2011236–241,_288–294-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though attending Christian Sunday services, Mandela studied Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301,_313Sampson2011232_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301,_313Sampson2011232-155">[154]</a></sup> He also studied <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, hoping to build a mutual respect with the warders and convert them to his cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010295,_299–301Sampson2011229_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010295,_299–301Sampson2011229-156">[155]</a></sup> Various official visitors met with Mandela, most significantly the liberal parliamentary representative <a href="/wiki/Helen_Suzman" title="Helen Suzman">Helen Suzman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(South_Africa)" title="Progressive Party (South Africa)">Progressive Party</a>, who championed Mandela's cause outside of prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301–302Sampson2011221_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301–302Sampson2011221-157">[156]</a></sup> In September 1970, he met <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Labour_Party" title="British Labour Party">British Labour Party</a> politician <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010337Sampson2011222_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010337Sampson2011222-158">[157]</a></sup> South African Minister of Justice <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Kruger" title="Jimmy Kruger">Jimmy Kruger</a> visited in December 1974, but he and Mandela did not get along with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010334Sampson2011241_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010334Sampson2011241-159">[158]</a></sup> His mother visited in 1968, dying shortly after, and his firstborn son Thembi died in a car accident the following year; Mandela was forbidden from attending either funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006142,_145Meredith2010303–304Sampson2011246–247_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006142,_145Meredith2010303–304Sampson2011246–247-160">[159]</a></sup> His wife was rarely able to see him, being regularly imprisoned for political activity, and his daughters first visited in December 1975. Winnie was released from prison in 1977 but was forcibly settled in <a href="/wiki/Brandfort" title="Brandfort">Brandfort</a> and remained unable to see him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986192–194Meer1988306–307Meredith2010287–288,_304–310Sampson2011248–254,_302_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986192–194Meer1988306–307Meredith2010287–288,_304–310Sampson2011248–254,_302-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1967 onwards, prison conditions improved. Black prisoners were given trousers rather than shorts, games were permitted, and the standard of their food was raised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301Sampson2011222,_235_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010301Sampson2011222,_235-162">[161]</a></sup> In 1969, an escape plan for Mandela was developed by Gordon Bruce, but it was abandoned after the conspiracy was infiltrated by an agent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_Bureau_of_State_Security" title="South African Bureau of State Security">South African Bureau of State Security</a> (BOSS), who hoped to see Mandela shot during the escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988207–208Sampson2011231_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988207–208Sampson2011231-163">[162]</a></sup> In 1970, Commander Piet Badenhorst became commanding officer. Mandela, seeing an increase in the physical and mental abuse of prisoners, complained to visiting judges, who had Badenhorst reassigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006127–128Meredith2010308–310Sampson2011223–225_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006127–128Meredith2010308–310Sampson2011223–225-164">[163]</a></sup> He was replaced by Commander Willie Willemse, who developed a co-operative relationship with Mandela and was keen to improve prison standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006128–129Sampson2011226–227_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006128–129Sampson2011226–227-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Robben_Island_f%C3%A6ngslet_Sektion_B.jpg/205px-Robben_Island_f%C3%A6ngslet_Sektion_B.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Robben_Island_f%C3%A6ngslet_Sektion_B.jpg/410px-Robben_Island_f%C3%A6ngslet_Sektion_B.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The inside of Mandela's prison cell as it was when he was imprisoned in 1964 and his open cell window facing the prison yard on Robben Island, now a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_heritage_sites_(South_Africa)" title="National heritage sites (South Africa)">national</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>. Mandela's cell later contained more furniture, including a bed from around 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton199460_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton199460-166">[165]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>By 1975, Mandela had become a Class A prisoner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011228_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011228-167">[166]</a></sup> which allowed him greater numbers of visits and letters. He corresponded with anti-apartheid activists like <a href="/wiki/Mangosuthu_Buthelezi" title="Mangosuthu Buthelezi">Mangosuthu Buthelezi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011314–315_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011314–315-168">[167]</a></sup> That year, he began his autobiography, which was smuggled to London, but remained unpublished at the time; prison authorities discovered several pages, and his LLB study privileges were revoked for four years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988268Lodge2006139Meredith2010317Sampson2011242–243_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988268Lodge2006139Meredith2010317Sampson2011242–243-169">[168]</a></sup> Instead, he devoted his spare time to gardening and reading until the authorities permitted him to resume his LLB degree studies in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011285–286_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011285–286-170">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 1960s, Mandela's fame had been eclipsed by <a href="/wiki/Steve_Biko" title="Steve Biko">Steve Biko</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Consciousness_Movement" title="Black Consciousness Movement">Black Consciousness Movement</a> (BCM). Seeing the ANC as ineffectual, the BCM called for militant action, but, following the <a href="/wiki/Soweto_uprising" title="Soweto uprising">Soweto uprising</a> of 1976, many BCM activists were imprisoned on Robben Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986186–188Meer1988304–306Meredith2010324–327Sampson2011259–276_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986186–188Meer1988304–306Meredith2010324–327Sampson2011259–276-171">[170]</a></sup> Mandela tried to build a relationship with these young radicals, although he was critical of their racialism and contempt for white anti-apartheid activists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006135Meredith2010327–328Sampson2011277–283Soudien2015363–364_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006135Meredith2010327–328Sampson2011277–283Soudien2015363–364-172">[171]</a></sup> Renewed international interest in his plight came in July 1978, when he celebrated his 60th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011296_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011296-173">[172]</a></sup> He was awarded an honorary doctorate in <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru_Award" title="Jawaharlal Nehru Award">Jawaharlal Nehru Award for International Understanding</a> in India in 1979, and the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">Freedom of the City</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, Scotland in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988313,_314Sampson2011315–316_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988313,_314Sampson2011315–316-174">[173]</a></sup> In March 1980, the slogan "Free Mandela!" was developed by journalist <a href="/wiki/Percy_Qoboza" title="Percy Qoboza">Percy Qoboza</a>, sparking an international campaign that led the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a> to call for his release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006155Meredith2010338–339Sampson2011319–320_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006155Meredith2010338–339Sampson2011319–320-175">[174]</a></sup> Despite increasing foreign pressure, the government refused, relying on its <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> allies US president <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>; both considered Mandela's ANC a terrorist organisation sympathetic to communism and supported its suppression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200424Sampson2011321_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200424Sampson2011321-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Pollsmoor_Prison:_1982.E2.80.931988"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollsmoor_Prison:_1982–1988">Pollsmoor Prison: 1982–1988</span></h4>
<p>In April 1982, Mandela was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Pollsmoor_Prison" title="Pollsmoor Prison">Pollsmoor Prison</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tokai,_Cape_Town" title="Tokai, Cape Town">Tokai, Cape Town</a>, along with senior ANC leaders Walter Sisulu, Andrew Mlangeni, <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Kathrada" title="Ahmed Kathrada">Ahmed Kathrada</a> and Raymond Mhlaba; they believed that they were being isolated to remove their influence on younger activists at Robben Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986218Lodge2006147–149Meredith2010340Sampson2011324–325_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986218Lodge2006147–149Meredith2010340Sampson2011324–325-177">[176]</a></sup> Conditions at Pollsmoor were better than at Robben Island, although Mandela missed the camaraderie and scenery of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006148Meredith2010346–347Sampson2011324–325_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006148Meredith2010346–347Sampson2011324–325-178">[177]</a></sup> Getting on well with Pollsmoor's commanding officer, Brigadier Munro, Mandela was permitted to create a <a href="/wiki/Roof_garden" title="Roof garden">roof garden</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010347Sampson2011326_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010347Sampson2011326-179">[178]</a></sup> he also read voraciously and corresponded widely, now being permitted 52 letters a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011329_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011329-180">[179]</a></sup> He was appointed patron of the multi-racial <a href="/wiki/United_Democratic_Front_(South_Africa)" title="United Democratic Front (South Africa)">United Democratic Front</a> (UDF), founded to combat <a href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1983" title="South African Constitution of 1983">reforms</a> implemented by South African president <a href="/wiki/P._W._Botha" title="P. W. Botha">P. W. Botha</a>. Botha's National Party government had permitted Coloured and Indian citizens to vote for their own parliaments, which had control over education, health and housing, but black Africans were excluded from the system. Like Mandela, the UDF saw this as an attempt to divide the anti-apartheid movement on racial lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986224Barber200420,_23,_26–27Meredith2010341–346Sampson2011335–336_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986224Barber200420,_23,_26–27Meredith2010341–346Sampson2011335–336-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2467" data-file-width="1503" decoding="async" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg/220px-Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg/330px-Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg/440px-Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_Bust_at_Southbank.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Mandela erected on London's <a href="/wiki/South_Bank" title="South Bank">South Bank</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Council" title="Greater London Council">Greater London Council</a> administration of <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a> in 1985</div></div></div>
<p>The early 1980s witnessed an escalation of violence across the country, and many predicted civil war. This was accompanied by economic stagnation as various multinational banks—under pressure from an international lobby—had stopped investing in South Africa. Numerous banks and Thatcher asked Botha to release Mandela—then at the height of his international fame—to defuse the volatile situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986247–248Barber200430Lodge2006152–153,_156Meredith2010249–256Sampson2011338–342_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986247–248Barber200430Lodge2006152–153,_156Meredith2010249–256Sampson2011338–342-182">[181]</a></sup> Although considering Mandela a dangerous "arch-Marxist",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986210Meredith2010340_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986210Meredith2010340-183">[182]</a></sup> Botha offered him, in February 1985, a release from prison if he "unconditionally rejected violence as a political weapon". Mandela spurned the offer, releasing a statement through his daughter Zindzi stating, "What freedom am I being offered while the organisation of the people [ANC] remains banned? Only free men can negotiate. A prisoner cannot enter into contracts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986237–238Meer1988315–318Barber200436Lodge2006157Meredith2010351–352Sampson2011330–332_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986237–238Meer1988315–318Barber200436Lodge2006157Meredith2010351–352Sampson2011330–332-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1985, Mandela underwent surgery on an enlarged prostate gland before being given new solitary quarters on the ground floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986254Lodge2006157–158Meredith2010358Sampson2011343–345_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986254Lodge2006157–158Meredith2010358Sampson2011343–345-186">[185]</a></sup> He was met by "seven eminent persons", an international delegation sent to negotiate a settlement, but Botha's government refused to co-operate, calling a state of emergency in June and initiating a police crackdown on unrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010359–360Sampson2011347–355_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010359–360Sampson2011347–355-187">[186]</a></sup> The anti-apartheid resistance fought back, with the ANC committing 231 attacks in 1986 and 235 in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011355_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011355-188">[187]</a></sup> The violence escalated as the government used the army and police to combat the resistance and provided covert support for vigilante groups and the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a> nationalist movement <a href="/wiki/Inkatha_Freedom_Party" title="Inkatha Freedom Party">Inkatha</a>, which was involved in an increasingly violent struggle with the ANC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011354–357_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011354–357-189">[188]</a></sup> Mandela requested talks with Botha but was denied, instead secretly meeting with Minister of Justice <a href="/wiki/Kobie_Coetsee" title="Kobie Coetsee">Kobie Coetsee</a> in 1987, and having a further 11 meetings over the next three years. Coetsee organised negotiations between Mandela and a team of four government figures starting in May 1988; the team agreed to the release of political prisoners and the legalisation of the ANC on the condition that they permanently renounce violence, break links with the Communist Party, and not insist on <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority rule</a>. Mandela rejected these conditions, insisting that the ANC would end its armed activities only when the government renounced violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006160Meredith2010362–368Sampson2011363–378_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006160Meredith2010362–368Sampson2011363–378-190">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela's 70th birthday in July 1988 attracted international attention, including <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela_70th_Birthday_Tribute" title="Nelson Mandela 70th Birthday Tribute">a tribute concert</a> at London's <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium_(1923)" title="Wembley Stadium (1923)">Wembley Stadium</a> that was televised and watched by an estimated 200 million viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200435Sampson2011368_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200435Sampson2011368-191">[190]</a></sup> Although presented globally as a heroic figure, he faced personal problems when ANC leaders informed him that Winnie had set herself up as head of a gang, the "Mandela United Football Club," which had been responsible for torturing and killing opponents—including children—in Soweto. Though some encouraged him to divorce her, he decided to remain loyal until she was found guilty by trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198820–23Lodge2006183–184Meredith2010371–383Sampson2011373–380_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198820–23Lodge2006183–184Meredith2010371–383Sampson2011373–380-192">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Victor_Verster_Prison_and_release:_1988.E2.80.931990"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Victor_Verster_Prison_and_release:_1988–1990">Victor Verster Prison and release: 1988–1990</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016,_Berlin,_Weltgewerkschaftskongress,_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="785" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016%2C_Berlin%2C_Weltgewerkschaftskongress%2C_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1986-0920-016,_Berlin,_Weltgewerkschaftskongress,_Probe_des_Festprogramms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Free Mandela" protest in <a href="/wiki/East_Berlin" title="East Berlin">East Berlin</a>, 1986</div></div></div>
<p>Recovering from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> exacerbated by the damp conditions in his cell,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988318–319Lodge2006160Meredith2010369Sampson2011369–370_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988318–319Lodge2006160Meredith2010369Sampson2011369–370-193">[192]</a></sup> Mandela was moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Verster_Prison" title="Victor Verster Prison">Victor Verster Prison</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Paarl" title="Paarl">Paarl</a>, in December 1988. He was housed in the relative comfort of a warder's house with a personal cook, and he used the time to complete his LLB degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988320Lodge2006160Meredith2010369–370Sampson2011381_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988320Lodge2006160Meredith2010369–370Sampson2011381-194">[193]</a></sup> While there, he was permitted many visitors and organised secret communications with exiled ANC leader Oliver Tambo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011384–385,_392–393_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011384–385,_392–393-195">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1989, Botha suffered a stroke; although he would retain the state presidency, he stepped down as leader of the National Party, to be replaced by <a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">F. W. de Klerk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200441Lodge200662Meredith2010388Sampson2011386_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200441Lodge200662Meredith2010388Sampson2011386-197">[196]</a></sup> In a surprise move, Botha invited Mandela to a meeting over tea in July 1989, an invitation Mandela considered genial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006161–162Meredith2010387–388Sampson2011390–392_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006161–162Meredith2010387–388Sampson2011390–392-198">[197]</a></sup> Botha was replaced as state president by de Klerk six weeks later; the new president believed that apartheid was unsustainable and released a number of ANC prisoners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200441–42Sampson2011392–397_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200441–42Sampson2011392–397-199">[198]</a></sup> Following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> in November 1989, de Klerk called his cabinet together to debate legalising the ANC and freeing Mandela. Although some were deeply opposed to his plans, de Klerk met with Mandela in December to discuss the situation, a meeting both men considered friendly, before legalising all formerly banned political parties in February 1990 and announcing Mandela's unconditional release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997567Barber20041Lodge2006165–166Meredith2010369–397Sampson2011399–402_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997567Barber20041Lodge2006165–166Meredith2010369–397Sampson2011399–402-200">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, for the first time in 20 years, photographs of Mandela were allowed to be published in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011403_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011403-202">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>Leaving Victor Verster Prison on 11 February, Mandela held Winnie's hand in front of amassed crowds and the press; the event was broadcast live across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006167Meredith2010399–402Sampson2011407_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006167Meredith2010399–402Sampson2011407-203">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> Driven to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town_City_Hall" title="Cape Town City Hall">Cape Town's City Hall</a> through crowds, he gave a speech declaring his commitment to peace and reconciliation with the white minority, but he made it clear that the ANC's armed struggle was not over and would continue as "a purely defensive action against the violence of apartheid". He expressed hope that the government would agree to negotiations, so that "there may no longer be the need for the armed struggle", and insisted that his main focus was to bring peace to the black majority and give them the right to vote in national and local elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber20042Meredith2010400–402Sampson2011408–409_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber20042Meredith2010400–402Sampson2011408–409-205">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> Staying at Tutu's home, in the following days Mandela met with friends, activists, and press, giving a speech to an estimated 100,000 people at Johannesburg's <a href="/wiki/FNB_Stadium" title="FNB Stadium">FNB Stadium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006171Meredith2010403–405Sampson2011409–410_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006171Meredith2010403–405Sampson2011409–410-207">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_apartheid">End of apartheid</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Negotiations_to_end_apartheid_in_South_Africa" title="Negotiations to end apartheid in South Africa">Negotiations to end apartheid in South Africa</a></div>
<h4><span id="Early_negotiations:_1990.E2.80.9391"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_negotiations:_1990–91">Early negotiations: 1990–91</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luthuli_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="2136" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Luthuli_House.jpg/170px-Luthuli_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Luthuli_House.jpg/255px-Luthuli_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Luthuli_House.jpg/340px-Luthuli_House.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luthuli_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Luthuli_House" title="Luthuli House">Luthuli House</a> in Johannesburg, which became the ANC headquarters in 1991</div></div></div>
<p>Mandela proceeded on an African tour, meeting supporters and politicians in Zambia, Zimbabwe, Namibia, Libya and Algeria, and continuing to Sweden, where he was reunited with Tambo, and London, where he appeared at the <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela:_An_International_Tribute_for_a_Free_South_Africa" title="Nelson Mandela: An International Tribute for a Free South Africa">Nelson Mandela: An International Tribute for a Free South Africa</a> concert at <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Stadium_(1923)" title="Wembley Stadium (1923)">Wembley Stadium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200457Lodge2006172Meredith2010409–410Sampson2011412–414_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200457Lodge2006172Meredith2010409–410Sampson2011412–414-208">[207]</a></sup> Encouraging foreign countries to support sanctions against the apartheid government, he met President <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a> in France, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> in the Vatican, and Thatcher in the United Kingdom. In the United States, he met President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, addressed both Houses of Congress and visited eight cities, being particularly popular among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African-American</a> community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200458–59Sampson2011415–418_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200458–59Sampson2011415–418-209">[208]</a></sup> In Cuba, he became friends with President Castro, whom he had long admired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200460Meredith2010410Sampson2011420_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200460Meredith2010410Sampson2011420-210">[209]</a></sup> He met President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R._Venkataraman" title="R. Venkataraman">R. Venkataraman</a> in India, President <a href="/wiki/Suharto" title="Suharto">Suharto</a> in Indonesia, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Mahathir_Mohamad" title="Mahathir Mohamad">Mahathir Mohamad</a> in Malaysia, and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hawke" title="Bob Hawke">Bob Hawke</a> in Australia. He visited Japan, but not the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, a longtime ANC supporter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200460Sampson2011418–420_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200460Sampson2011418–420-211">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1990, Mandela led a multiracial ANC delegation into preliminary negotiations with a government delegation of 11 Afrikaner men. Mandela impressed them with his discussions of Afrikaner history, and the negotiations led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groote_Schuur_Minute" title="Groote Schuur Minute">Groot Schuur Minute</a>, in which the government lifted the state of emergency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010412–413Sampson2011424–427_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010412–413Sampson2011424–427-212">[211]</a></sup> In August, Mandela—recognising the ANC's severe military disadvantage—offered a ceasefire, the Pretoria Minute, for which he was widely criticised by MK activists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010412–413Sampson2011424–427_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010412–413Sampson2011424–427-212">[211]</a></sup> He spent much time trying to unify and build the ANC, appearing at a Johannesburg conference in December attended by 1,600 delegates, many of whom found him more moderate than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010439Sampson2011428–429_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010439Sampson2011428–429-213">[212]</a></sup> At the ANC's July 1991 national conference in Durban, Mandela admitted that the party had faults and announced his aim to build a "strong and well-oiled task force" for securing majority rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200447Lodge2006173Meredith2010439–440Sampson2011429–430_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200447Lodge2006173Meredith2010439–440Sampson2011429–430-214">[213]</a></sup> At the conference, he was elected ANC President, replacing the ailing Tambo, and a 50-strong multiracial, mixed gendered national executive was elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200447Lodge2006173Meredith2010439–440Sampson2011429–430_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200447Lodge2006173Meredith2010439–440Sampson2011429–430-214">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela was given an office in the newly purchased ANC headquarters at <a href="/wiki/Shell_House_(Johannesburg)" title="Shell House (Johannesburg)">Shell House</a>, Johannesburg, and moved into Winnie's large Soweto home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010409Sampson2011431,_448_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010409Sampson2011431,_448-215">[214]</a></sup> Their marriage was increasingly strained as he learned of her affair with Dali Mpofu, but he supported her during her trial for kidnapping and assault. He gained funding for her defence from the International Defence and Aid Fund for Southern Africa and from Libyan leader <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a>, but, in June 1991, she was found guilty and sentenced to six years in prison, reduced to two on appeal. On 13 April 1992, Mandela publicly announced his separation from Winnie. The ANC forced her to step down from the national executive for misappropriating ANC funds; Mandela moved into the mostly white Johannesburg suburb of <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Estate" title="Houghton Estate">Houghton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010429–436,_435–460Sampson2011431,_448_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010429–436,_435–460Sampson2011431,_448-216">[215]</a></sup> Mandela's prospects for a peaceful transition were further damaged by an increase in "black-on-black" violence, particularly between ANC and Inkatha supporters in <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a>, which resulted in thousands of deaths. Mandela met with Inkatha leader Buthelezi, but the ANC prevented further negotiations on the issue. Mandela argued that there was a "<a href="/wiki/Third_Force_(South_Africa)" title="Third Force (South Africa)">third force</a>" within the state intelligence services fuelling the "slaughter of the people" and openly blamed de Klerk—whom he increasingly distrusted—for the <a href="/wiki/Sebokeng" title="Sebokeng">Sebokeng</a> massacre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETomaselliTomaselli20036Lodge2006174Meredith2010418–424Sampson2011436–442_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETomaselliTomaselli20036Lodge2006174Meredith2010418–424Sampson2011436–442-217">[216]</a></sup> In September 1991, a national peace conference was held in Johannesburg at which Mandela, Buthelezi and de Klerk signed a peace accord, though the violence continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010425–426Sampson2011444_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010425–426Sampson2011444-218">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="CODESA_talks:_1991.E2.80.9392"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="CODESA_talks:_1991–92">CODESA talks: 1991–92</span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_for_a_Democratic_South_Africa" title="Convention for a Democratic South Africa">Convention for a Democratic South Africa</a> (CODESA) began in December 1991 at the Johannesburg World Trade Centre, attended by 228 delegates from 19 political parties. Although <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Ramaphosa" title="Cyril Ramaphosa">Cyril Ramaphosa</a> led the ANC's delegation, Mandela remained a key figure. After de Klerk used the closing speech to condemn the ANC's violence, he took to the stage to denounce de Klerk as the "head of an illegitimate, discredited minority regime". Dominated by the National Party and ANC, little negotiation was achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200445,_69Lodge2006174–175Meredith2010443–446Sampson2011456–459_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200445,_69Lodge2006174–175Meredith2010443–446Sampson2011456–459-219">[218]</a></sup> CODESA 2 was held in May 1992, at which de Klerk insisted that post-apartheid South Africa must use a <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federal system</a> with a rotating presidency to ensure the protection of ethnic minorities; Mandela opposed this, demanding a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary system</a> governed by majority rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Boipatong_massacre" title="Boipatong massacre">Boipatong massacre</a> of ANC activists by government-aided Inkatha militants, Mandela called off the negotiations, before attending a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity</a> in Senegal, at which he called for a special session of the UN Security Council and proposed that a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_peacekeeping" title="United Nations peacekeeping">UN peacekeeping force</a> be stationed in South Africa to prevent "<a href="/wiki/State_terrorism" title="State terrorism">state terrorism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200472–73Lodge2006177Meredith2010462–463Sampson2011461–462_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200472–73Lodge2006177Meredith2010462–463Sampson2011461–462-221">[220]</a></sup> Calling for domestic mass action, in August the ANC organised the largest-ever strike in South African history, and supporters marched on Pretoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/220px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/330px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg/440px-Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frederik_de_Klerk_with_Nelson_Mandela_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_1992.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">De Klerk</a> and Mandela at the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>, 1992</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Bisho_massacre" title="Bisho massacre">Bisho massacre</a>, in which 28 ANC supporters and one soldier were shot dead by the <a href="/wiki/Ciskei_Defence_Force" title="Ciskei Defence Force">Ciskei Defence Force</a> during a protest march, Mandela realised that mass action was leading to further violence and resumed negotiations in September. He agreed to do so on the conditions that all political prisoners be released, that Zulu traditional weapons be banned, and that Zulu hostels would be fenced off, the latter two measures intended to prevent further Inkatha attacks; de Klerk reluctantly agreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006177–178Meredith2010467–471Sampson2011463–466_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006177–178Meredith2010467–471Sampson2011463–466-223">[222]</a></sup> The negotiations agreed that a multiracial general election would be held, resulting in a five-year <a href="/wiki/National_unity_government" title="National unity government">coalition government of national unity</a> and a constitutional assembly that gave the National Party continuing influence. The ANC also conceded to safeguarding the jobs of white civil servants; such concessions brought fierce internal criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200479Lodge2006180Sampson2011467–468_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200479Lodge2006180Sampson2011467–468-224">[223]</a></sup> The duo agreed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1993" title="South African Constitution of 1993">an interim constitution</a> based on a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democratic</a> model, guaranteeing separation of powers, creating a constitutional court, and including a US-style <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a>; it also divided the country into nine provinces, each with its own <a href="/wiki/Premier" title="Premier">premier</a> and civil service, a concession between de Klerk's desire for <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a> and Mandela's for unitary government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200480Meredith2010489–491Sampson2011472_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200480Meredith2010489–491Sampson2011472-225">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>The democratic process was threatened by the Concerned South Africans Group (COSAG), an alliance of black ethnic-secessionist groups like Inkatha and far-right Afrikaner parties; in June 1993, one of the latter—the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner_Weerstandsbeweging" title="Afrikaner Weerstandsbeweging">Afrikaner Weerstandsbeweging</a> (AWB)—<a href="/wiki/Storming_of_Kempton_Park_World_Trade_Centre" title="Storming of Kempton Park World Trade Centre">attacked the Kempton Park World Trade Centre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200446Meredith2010449–450,_488Sampson2011466,_470–471_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200446Meredith2010449–450,_488Sampson2011466,_470–471-226">[225]</a></sup> Following the murder of ANC activist <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hani" title="Chris Hani">Chris Hani</a>, Mandela made a publicised speech to calm rioting, soon after appearing at a mass funeral in Soweto for Tambo, who had died of a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006180–181Meredith2010476–480Sampson2011468–469_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006180–181Meredith2010476–480Sampson2011468–469-227">[226]</a></sup> In July 1993, both Mandela and de Klerk visited the United States, independently meeting President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, and each receiving the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Medal" title="Liberty Medal">Liberty Medal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011471_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011471-228">[227]</a></sup> Soon after, Mandela and de Klerk were jointly awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200468Lodge2006182Meredith2010494Sampson2011474_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200468Lodge2006182Meredith2010494Sampson2011474-229">[228]</a></sup> Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a>, Mandela began meeting with big business figures, and he played down his support for nationalisation, fearing that he would scare away much-needed foreign investment. Although criticised by socialist ANC members, he had been encouraged to embrace private enterprise by members of the Chinese and Vietnamese Communist parties at the January 1992 <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> in Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006182Meredith2010497Sampson2011434–445,_473_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006182Meredith2010497Sampson2011434–445,_473-230">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election:_1994">General election: 1994</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1994_South_African_general_election" title="1994 South African general election">1994 South African general election</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg/170px-Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg/255px-Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg/340px-Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandela_voting_in_1994.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela casting his vote in the <a href="/wiki/1994_South_African_general_election" title="1994 South African general election">1994 election</a></div></div></div>
<p>With the election set for 27 April 1994, the ANC began campaigning, opening 100 election offices and orchestrating People's Forums across the country at which Mandela could appear, as a popular figure with great status among black South Africans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010495Sampson2011467–477_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010495Sampson2011467–477-231">[230]</a></sup> The ANC campaigned on a <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_and_Development_Programme" title="Reconstruction and Development Programme">Reconstruction and Development Programme</a> (RDP) to build a million houses in five years, introduce universal free education and extend access to water and electricity. The party's slogan was "a better life for all", although it was not explained how this development would be funded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200476–77Meredith2010495–496Sampson2011478_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200476–77Meredith2010495–496Sampson2011478-232">[231]</a></sup> With the exception of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weekly_Mail" title="Weekly Mail">Weekly Mail</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Nation" title="New Nation">New Nation</a></i>, South Africa's press opposed Mandela's election, fearing continued ethnic strife, instead supporting the National or <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(South_Africa)" title="Democratic Party (South Africa)">Democratic Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011479_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011479-233">[232]</a></sup> Mandela devoted much time to fundraising for the ANC, touring North America, Europe and Asia to meet wealthy donors, including former supporters of the apartheid regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011-234">[233]</a></sup> He also urged a reduction in the voting age from 18 to 14; rejected by the ANC, this policy became the subject of ridicule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006188Sampson2011477–478Meredith2010484_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006188Sampson2011477–478Meredith2010484-235">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerned that COSAG would undermine the election, particularly in the wake of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bophuthatswana_conflict_(1994)" title="Bophuthatswana conflict (1994)">conflict in Bophuthatswana</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shell_House_massacre" title="Shell House massacre">Shell House massacre</a>—incidents of violence involving the AWB and Inkatha, respectively—Mandela met with Afrikaner politicians and generals, including P. W. Botha, <a href="/wiki/Pik_Botha" title="Pik Botha">Pik Botha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constand_Viljoen" title="Constand Viljoen">Constand Viljoen</a>, persuading many to work within the democratic system. With de Klerk, he also convinced Inkatha's Buthelezi to enter the elections rather than launch a war of secession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010488–489,_504–510Sampson2011480–489_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010488–489,_504–510Sampson2011480–489-236">[235]</a></sup> As leaders of the two major parties, de Klerk and Mandela appeared on a televised debate; although de Klerk was widely considered the better speaker at the event, Mandela's offer to shake his hand surprised him, leading some commentators to deem it a victory for Mandela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010500–501Sampson2011488_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010500–501Sampson2011488-237">[236]</a></sup> The election went ahead with little violence, although an AWB cell killed 20 with car bombs. As widely expected, the ANC won a sweeping victory, taking 63% of the vote, just short of the two-thirds majority needed to unilaterally change the constitution. The ANC was also victorious in seven provinces, with Inkatha and the National Party each taking one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200482Meredith2010512Sampson2011491_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200482Meredith2010512Sampson2011491-238">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup> Mandela voted at the <a href="/wiki/Ohlange_High_School" title="Ohlange High School">Ohlange High School</a> in Durban, and though the ANC's victory assured his election as president, he publicly accepted that the election had been marred by instances of fraud and sabotage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010510–512Sampson2011490_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010510–512Sampson2011490-240">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Presidency_of_South_Africa:_1994.E2.80.931999"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_of_South_Africa:_1994–1999">Presidency of South Africa: 1994–1999</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Nelson_Mandela" title="Presidency of Nelson Mandela">Presidency of Nelson Mandela</a></div>
<p>The newly elected National Assembly's first act was to formally elect Mandela as South Africa's first black chief executive. His inauguration took place in Pretoria on 10 May 1994, televised to a billion viewers globally. The event was attended by four thousand guests, including world leaders from a wide range of geographic and ideological backgrounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010514Sampson2011492–493_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010514Sampson2011492–493-242">[241]</a></sup> Mandela headed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(South_Africa)" title="Government of National Unity (South Africa)">Government of National Unity</a> dominated by the ANC—which had no experience of governing by itself—but containing representatives from the National Party and Inkatha. Under the Interim Constitution, Inkatha and the National Party were entitled to seats in the government by virtue of winning at least 20 seats. In keeping with earlier agreements, both de Klerk and Thabo Mbeki were given the position of <a href="/wiki/Deputy_President_of_South_Africa" title="Deputy President of South Africa">Deputy President</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber20043Sampson2011491–492_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber20043Sampson2011491–492-243">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup> Although Mbeki had not been his first choice for the job, Mandela grew to rely heavily on him throughout his presidency, allowing him to shape policy details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200487Lodge2006210Meredith2010566Sampson2011508–511_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200487Lodge2006210Meredith2010566Sampson2011508–511-245">[244]</a></sup> Moving into the presidential office at <a href="/wiki/Tuynhuys" title="Tuynhuys">Tuynhuys</a> in Cape Town, Mandela allowed de Klerk to retain the presidential residence in the <a href="/wiki/Groote_Schuur" title="Groote Schuur">Groote Schuur</a> estate, instead settling into the nearby Westbrooke manor, which he renamed "<a href="/wiki/Genadendal_Residence" title="Genadendal Residence">Genadendal</a>", meaning "Valley of Mercy" in Afrikaans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010523,_543Sampson2011496–497_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010523,_543Sampson2011496–497-246">[245]</a></sup> Retaining his Houghton home, he also had a house built in his home village of Qunu, which he visited regularly, walking around the area, meeting with locals, and judging tribal disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011502_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011502-247">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aged 76, he faced various ailments, and although exhibiting continued energy, he felt isolated and lonely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011497–499,_510_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011497–499,_510-248">[247]</a></sup> He often entertained celebrities, such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whoopi_Goldberg" title="Whoopi Goldberg">Whoopi Goldberg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Spice_Girls" title="Spice Girls">Spice Girls</a>, and befriended ultra-rich businessmen, like <a href="/wiki/Harry_Oppenheimer" title="Harry Oppenheimer">Harry Oppenheimer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Anglo_American_plc" title="Anglo American plc">Anglo American</a>. He also met with <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> on her March 1995 <a href="/wiki/State_visit" title="State visit">state visit</a> to South Africa, which earned him strong criticism from ANC anti-capitalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011501,_504_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011501,_504-249">[248]</a></sup> Despite his opulent surroundings, Mandela lived simply, donating a third of his <a href="/wiki/South_African_rand" title="South African rand">R</a> 552,000 annual income to the <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela_Children%27s_Fund" title="Nelson Mandela Children's Fund">Nelson Mandela Children's Fund</a>, which he had founded in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006209Meredith2010543Sampson2011517_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006209Meredith2010543Sampson2011517-250">[249]</a></sup> Although dismantling press censorship, speaking out in favour of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a> and befriending many journalists, Mandela was critical of much of the country's media, noting that it was overwhelmingly owned and run by middle-class whites and believing that it focused too heavily on scaremongering about crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006208–209Meredith2010547–548Sampson2011525–527_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006208–209Meredith2010547–548Sampson2011525–527-251">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1994, Mandela published <i><a href="/wiki/Long_Walk_to_Freedom" title="Long Walk to Freedom">Long Walk to Freedom</a></i>, an autobiography based around a manuscript he had written in prison, augmented by interviews conducted with American journalist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stengel" title="Richard Stengel">Richard Stengel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006186Meredith2010517_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006186Meredith2010517-252">[251]</a></sup> In late 1994, he attended the 49th conference of the ANC in <a href="/wiki/Bloemfontein" title="Bloemfontein">Bloemfontein</a>, at which a more militant national executive was elected, among them Winnie Mandela; although she expressed an interest in reconciling, Nelson initiated divorce proceedings in August 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010539–542Sampson2011500,_507_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010539–542Sampson2011500,_507-253">[252]</a></sup> By 1995, he had entered into a relationship with <a href="/wiki/Gra%C3%A7a_Machel" title="Graça Machel">Graça Machel</a>, a Mozambican political activist 27 years his junior who was the widow of former president <a href="/wiki/Samora_Machel" title="Samora Machel">Samora Machel</a>. They had first met in July 1990 when she was still in mourning, but their friendship grew into a partnership, with Machel accompanying him on many of his foreign visits. She turned down Mandela's first marriage proposal, wanting to retain some independence and dividing her time between Mozambique and Johannesburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006222–223Meredith2010574–575Sampson2011546–549_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006222–223Meredith2010574–575Sampson2011546–549-254">[253]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_reconciliation">National reconciliation</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Gracious but steely, [Mandela] steered a country in turmoil toward a negotiated settlement: a country that days before its first democratic election remained violent, riven by divisive views and personalities. He endorsed national reconciliation, an idea he did not merely foster in the abstract, but performed with panache and conviction in reaching out to former adversaries. He initiated an era of hope that, while not long-lasting, was nevertheless decisive, and he garnered the highest international recognition and affection.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Rita Barnard, <i>The Cambridge Companion to Nelson Mandela</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard20141_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard20141-255">[254]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Presiding over the transition from apartheid minority rule to a multicultural democracy, Mandela saw national reconciliation as the primary task of his presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011524_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011524-256">[255]</a></sup> Having seen other post-colonial African economies damaged by the departure of white elites, Mandela worked to reassure South Africa's white population that they were protected and represented in "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainbow_Nation" title="Rainbow Nation">Rainbow Nation</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006213Meredith2010517Sampson2011495–496_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006213Meredith2010517Sampson2011495–496-257">[256]</a></sup> Although his Government of National Unity would be dominated by the ANC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200488Lodge2006204_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200488Lodge2006204-258">[257]</a></sup> he attempted to create a broad coalition by appointing de Klerk as Deputy President and appointing other National Party officials as ministers for Agriculture, Environment, and Minerals and Energy, as well as naming Buthelezi as Minister for Home Affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011507–511_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011507–511-259">[258]</a></sup> The other cabinet positions were taken by ANC members, many of whom—like <a href="/wiki/Joe_Modise" title="Joe Modise">Joe Modise</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Nzo" title="Alfred Nzo">Alfred Nzo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Slovo" title="Joe Slovo">Joe Slovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mac_Maharaj" title="Mac Maharaj">Mac Maharaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dullah_Omar" title="Dullah Omar">Dullah Omar</a>—had long been comrades of Mandela, although others, such as <a href="/wiki/Tito_Mboweni" title="Tito Mboweni">Tito Mboweni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Radebe" title="Jeff Radebe">Jeff Radebe</a>, were far younger.<sup class="reference"
