id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011508_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011508-260">[259]</a></sup> Mandela's relationship with de Klerk was strained; Mandela thought that de Klerk was intentionally provocative, and de Klerk felt that he was being intentionally humiliated by the president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006204–205Meredith2010528Sampson2011511,_534_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006204–205Meredith2010528Sampson2011511,_534-261">[260]</a></sup> In January 1995, Mandela heavily chastised de Klerk for awarding amnesty to 3,500 police officers just before the election, and later criticised him for defending former Minister of Defence <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Malan" title="Magnus Malan">Magnus Malan</a> when the latter was charged with murder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006204–205Meredith2010528Sampson2011511,_534_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006204–205Meredith2010528Sampson2011511,_534-261">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela personally met with senior figures of the apartheid regime, including lawyer <a href="/wiki/Percy_Yutar" title="Percy Yutar">Percy Yutar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Verwoerd" title="Hendrik Verwoerd">Hendrik Verwoerd</a>'s widow, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betsie_Schoombie" title="Betsie Schoombie">Betsie Schoombie</a>, also laying a wreath by the statue of Afrikaner hero <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Theron" title="Daniel Theron">Daniel Theron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006212Meredith2010523–524Sampson2011520,_522–523_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006212Meredith2010523–524Sampson2011520,_522–523-262">[261]</a></sup> Emphasising personal forgiveness and reconciliation, he announced that "courageous people do not fear forgiving, for the sake of peace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010523–524Sampson2011520,_522–523_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010523–524Sampson2011520,_522–523-263">[262]</a></sup> He encouraged black South Africans to get behind the previously hated national rugby team, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_national_rugby_union_team" title="South African national rugby union team">Springboks</a>, as South Africa hosted the <a href="/wiki/1995_Rugby_World_Cup" title="1995 Rugby World Cup">1995 Rugby World Cup</a>. Mandela wore a Springbok shirt at the <a href="/wiki/1995_Rugby_World_Cup_Final" title="1995 Rugby World Cup Final">final</a> against New Zealand, and after the Springboks won the match, Mandela presented the trophy to captain <a href="/wiki/Francois_Pienaar" title="Francois Pienaar">Francois Pienaar</a>, an Afrikaner. This was widely seen as a major step in the reconciliation of white and black South Africans; as de Klerk later put it, "Mandela won the hearts of millions of white rugby fans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006212Meredith2010525–527Sampson2011516,_524_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006212Meredith2010525–527Sampson2011516,_524-264">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[264]</a></sup> Mandela's efforts at reconciliation assuaged the fears of white people, but also drew criticism from more militant black people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006213Meredith2010517,_536Sampson2011491,_496,_524_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006213Meredith2010517,_536Sampson2011491,_496,_524-266">[265]</a></sup> Among the latter was his estranged wife, Winnie, who accused the ANC of being more interested in appeasing the white community than in helping the black majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010517,_536Sampson2011491,_496,_524_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010517,_536Sampson2011491,_496,_524-267">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela oversaw the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission_(South_Africa)" title="Truth and Reconciliation Commission (South Africa)">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</a> to investigate crimes committed under apartheid by both the government and the ANC, appointing Tutu as its chair. To prevent the creation of martyrs, the commission granted individual amnesties in exchange for testimony of crimes committed during the apartheid era. Dedicated in February 1996, it held two years of hearings detailing rapes, torture, bombings and assassinations before issuing its final report in October 1998. Both de Klerk and Mbeki appealed to have parts of the report suppressed, though only de Klerk's appeal was successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010527,_551–564Sampson2011528–532_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010527,_551–564Sampson2011528–532-268">[267]</a></sup> Mandela praised the commission's work, stating that it "had helped us move away from the past to concentrate on the present and the future".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010563Sampson2011532_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010563Sampson2011532-269">[268]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_programmes">Domestic programmes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rdphouses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2518" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rdphouses.jpg/220px-Rdphouses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rdphouses.jpg/330px-Rdphouses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rdphouses.jpg/440px-Rdphouses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rdphouses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Houses in Soweto constructed under the RDP program</div></div></div>
<p>Mandela's administration inherited a country with a huge disparity in wealth and services between white and black communities. Of a population of 40 million, around 23 million lacked electricity or adequate sanitation, and 12 million lacked clean water supplies, with 2 million children not in school and a third of the population illiterate. There was 33% unemployment, and just under half of the population lived below the poverty line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010518–520_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010518–520-270">[269]</a></sup> Government financial reserves were nearly depleted, with a fifth of the national budget being spent on debt repayment, meaning that the extent of the promised Reconstruction and Development Programme (RDP) was scaled back, with none of the proposed nationalisation or job creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010519Sampson2011514–515_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010519Sampson2011514–515-271">[270]</a></sup> In 1996, the RDP was replaced with a new policy, Growth, Employment and Redistribution (GEAR), which maintained South Africa's <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a> but placed an emphasis on economic growth through a framework of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_economics" title="Market economics">market economics</a> and the encouragement of foreign investment; many in the ANC derided it as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-liberalism" title="Neo-liberalism">neo-liberal</a> policy that did not address social inequality, no matter how Mandela defended it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004122–124,_162_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004122–124,_162-272">[271]</a></sup> In adopting this approach, Mandela's government adhered to the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_consensus" title="Washington consensus">Washington consensus</a>" advocated by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuthienKhosaMagubane2000369–370Meredith2010520–521_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuthienKhosaMagubane2000369–370Meredith2010520–521-273">[272]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="362" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG/220px-Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG/330px-Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_1998.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela on a visit to Brazil in 1998</div></div></div>
<p>Under Mandela's presidency, welfare spending increased by 13% in 1996/97, 13% in 1997/98, and 7% in 1998/99.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoustonMuthien200062_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoustonMuthien200062-274">[273]</a></sup> The government introduced parity in grants for communities, including disability grants, child maintenance grants and old-age pensions, which had previously been set at different levels for South Africa's different racial groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoustonMuthien200062_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoustonMuthien200062-274">[273]</a></sup> In 1994, free healthcare was introduced for children under six and pregnant women, a provision extended to all those using primary level public sector health care services in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006205Meredith2010521_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006205Meredith2010521-275">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[275]</a></sup> By the 1999 election, the ANC could boast that due to their policies, 3 million people were connected to telephone lines, 1.5 million children were brought into the education system, 500 clinics were upgraded or constructed, 2 million people were connected to the electricity grid, water access was extended to 3 million people, and 750,000 houses were constructed, housing nearly 3 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerbst2003312_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerbst2003312-277">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Land Reform Act 3 of 1996 safeguarded the rights of labour tenants living on farms where they grew crops or grazed livestock. This legislation ensured that such tenants could not be evicted without a court order or if they were over the age of 65.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> Recognising that arms manufacturing was a key industry for the South African economy, Mandela endorsed the trade in weapons but brought in tighter regulations surrounding <a href="/wiki/Armscor_(South_Africa)" title="Armscor (South Africa)">Armscor</a> to ensure that South African weaponry was not sold to authoritarian regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004102–194_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004102–194-279">[278]</a></sup> Under Mandela's administration, tourism was increasingly promoted, becoming a major sector of the South African economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004130_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004130-280">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics like <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Cameron" title="Edwin Cameron">Edwin Cameron</a> accused Mandela's government of doing little to stem the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> pandemic in the country; by 1999, 10% of South Africa's population were HIV positive. Mandela later admitted that he had personally neglected the issue, in part due to public reticence in discussing issues surrounding sex in South Africa, and that he had instead left the issue for Mbeki to deal with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004135–137Lodge2006219Meredith2010571–573_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004135–137Lodge2006219Meredith2010571–573-281">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[281]</a></sup> Mandela also received criticism for failing to sufficiently combat crime; South Africa had one of the world's highest crime rates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010573Sampson2011510,_565–68_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010573Sampson2011510,_565–68-283">[282]</a></sup> and the activities of international crime syndicates in the country grew significantly throughout the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004137–138_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004137–138-284">[283]</a></sup> Mandela's administration was also perceived as having failed to deal with the problem of corruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010544–547Sampson2011510_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010544–547Sampson2011510-285">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further problems were caused by the exodus of thousands of skilled white South Africans from the country, who were escaping the increasing crime rates, higher taxes and the impact of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_discrimination" title="Positive discrimination">positive discrimination</a> toward black people in employment. This exodus resulted in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_drain" title="Brain drain">brain drain</a>, and Mandela criticised those who left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004131Meredith2010573Sampson2011510,_565–68_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004131Meredith2010573Sampson2011510,_565–68-286">[285]</a></sup> At the same time, South Africa experienced an influx of millions of <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration" title="Illegal immigration">illegal migrants</a> from poorer parts of Africa; although public opinion toward these illegal immigrants was generally unfavourable, characterising them as disease-spreading criminals who were a drain on resources, Mandela called on South Africans to embrace them as "brothers and sisters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004133_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004133-287">[286]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h4>
<p>Mandela expressed the view that "South Africa's future foreign relations [should] be based on our belief that human rights should be the core of international relations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200489Lodge2006214_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200489Lodge2006214-288">[287]</a></sup> Following the South African example, Mandela encouraged other nations to resolve conflicts through diplomacy and reconciliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011555_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011555-289">[288]</a></sup> In September 1998, Mandela was appointed secretary-general of the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, who held their annual conference in Durban. He used the event to criticise the "narrow, chauvinistic interests" of the Israeli government in stalling negotiations to end the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a> and urged India and Pakistan to negotiate to end the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_conflict" title="Kashmir conflict">Kashmir conflict</a>, for which he was criticised by both Israel and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011559_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011559-290">[289]</a></sup> Inspired by the region's economic boom, Mandela sought greater economic relations with East Asia, in particular with Malaysia, although this was prevented by the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">1997 Asian financial crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[290]</a></sup> He extended diplomatic recognition to the People's Republic of China (PRC), who were growing as an economic force, and initially also to <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, who were already longstanding investors in the South African economy. However, under pressure from the PRC, he cut recognition of Taiwan in November 1996, and he paid an official visit to Beijing in May 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004107–108_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004107–108-292">[291]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2404" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg/220px-President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg/330px-President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg/440px-President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Bill_Clinton_with_Nelson_Mandela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela with US president <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>. Despite publicly criticising him on several occasions, Mandela liked Clinton, and personally supported him during <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Impeachment of Bill Clinton">his impeachment proceedings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006216Sampson2011561–567_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006216Sampson2011561–567-293">[292]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Mandela attracted controversy for his close relationship with Indonesian president <a href="/wiki/Suharto" title="Suharto">Suharto</a>, whose regime was responsible for mass human rights abuses, although on a July 1997 visit to Indonesia he privately urged Suharto to withdraw from the <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_occupation_of_East_Timor" title="Indonesian occupation of East Timor">occupation of East Timor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004104–105Sampson2011560_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004104–105Sampson2011560-294">[293]</a></sup> He also faced similar criticism from the West for his government's trade links to Syria, Cuba and Libya<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006214_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006214-295">[294]</a></sup> and for his personal friendships with Castro and Gaddafi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011562–563_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011562–563-296">[295]</a></sup> Castro visited South Africa in 1998 to widespread popular acclaim, and Mandela met Gaddafi in Libya to award him the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Good_Hope" title="Order of Good Hope">Order of Good Hope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011562–563_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011562–563-296">[295]</a></sup> When Western governments and media criticised these visits, Mandela lambasted such criticism as having racist undertones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011564_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011564-297">[296]</a></sup> and stated that "the enemies of countries in the West are not our enemies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006214_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006214-295">[294]</a></sup> Mandela hoped to resolve the long-running dispute between Libya and the United States and Britain over bringing to trial the two Libyans, <a href="/wiki/Abdelbaset_al-Megrahi" title="Abdelbaset al-Megrahi">Abdelbaset al-Megrahi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamin_Khalifah_Fhimah" title="Lamin Khalifah Fhimah">Lamin Khalifah Fhimah</a>, who were indicted in November 1991 and accused of sabotaging <a href="/wiki/Pan_Am_Flight_103" title="Pan Am Flight 103">Pan Am Flight 103</a>. Mandela proposed that they be tried in a third country, which was agreed to by all parties; governed by <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scots law</a>, the trial was held at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camp_Zeist,_Netherlands" title="Camp Zeist, Netherlands">Camp Zeist</a> in the Netherlands in April 1999, and found one of the two men guilty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004144Lodge2006215–216Sampson2011563–564_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004144Lodge2006215–216Sampson2011563–564-298">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela echoed Mbeki's calls for an "<a href="/wiki/African_Renaissance" title="African Renaissance">African Renaissance</a>", and he was greatly concerned with issues on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004124–125Sampson2011556–557_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004124–125Sampson2011556–557-300">[299]</a></sup> He took a <a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">soft diplomatic</a> approach to removing <a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Sani Abacha</a>'s military junta in Nigeria but later became a leading figure in calling for sanctions when Abacha's regime increased human rights violations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004108–110Lodge2006215Sampson2011556–557_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004108–110Lodge2006215Sampson2011556–557-301">[300]</a></sup> In 1996, he was appointed chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a> (SADC) and initiated unsuccessful negotiations to end the <a href="/wiki/First_Congo_War" title="First Congo War">First Congo War</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004176–177Lodge2006216Sampson2011558_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004176–177Lodge2006216Sampson2011558-302">[301]</a></sup> He also played a key role as a mediator in the ethnic conflict between <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> political groups in the <a href="/wiki/Burundian_Civil_War" title="Burundian Civil War">Burundian Civil War</a>, helping to initiate a settlement which brought increased stability to the country but did not end the ethnic violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006217–218_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006217–218-303">[302]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_intervention_in_Lesotho" title="South African intervention in Lesotho">South Africa's first post-apartheid military operation</a>, troops were ordered into Lesotho in September 1998 to protect the government of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Pakalitha_Mosisili" title="Pakalitha Mosisili">Pakalitha Mosisili</a> after a disputed election had prompted opposition uprisings. The action was not authorised by Mandela himself, who was out of the country at the time, but by Buthelezi, who was serving as acting president during Mandela's absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004111–113Sampson2011558–559_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004111–113Sampson2011558–559-304">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawing_from_politics">Withdrawing from politics</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="934" data-file-width="643" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg/150px-Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg/225px-Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg/300px-Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thabo-mbeki-district-six.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the latter part of his presidency, Mandela increasingly relied on his Deputy President, Thabo Mbeki (pictured)</div></div></div>
<p>The new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">Constitution of South Africa</a> was agreed upon by parliament in May 1996, enshrining a series of institutions to place checks on political and administrative authority within a constitutional democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuthienKhosaMagubane2000366_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuthienKhosaMagubane2000366-305">[304]</a></sup> De Klerk opposed the implementation of this constitution, and that month he and the National Party withdrew from the coalition government in protest, claiming that the ANC were not treating them as equals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004128–129Lodge2006204Meredith2010529–530Sampson2011534_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004128–129Lodge2006204Meredith2010529–530Sampson2011534-306">[305]</a></sup> The ANC took over the cabinet positions formerly held by the Nationalists, with Mbeki becoming sole Deputy President.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011535_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011535-307">[306]</a></sup> Inkatha remained part of the coalition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004128–129_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004128–129-308">[307]</a></sup> and when both Mandela and Mbeki were out of the country in September 1998, Buthelezi was appointed "Acting President", marking an improvement in his relationship with Mandela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006207Sampson2011536_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006207Sampson2011536-309">[308]</a></sup> Although Mandela had often governed decisively in his first two years as president,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006211_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006211-310">[309]</a></sup> he had subsequently increasingly delegated duties to Mbeki, retaining only a close personal supervision of intelligence and security measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2004130Lodge2006211_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2004130Lodge2006211-311">[310]</a></sup> During a 1997 visit to London, he said that "the ruler of South Africa, the <i>de facto</i> ruler, is Thabo Mbeki" and that he was "shifting everything to him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006211_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006211-310">[309]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela stepped down as ANC President at the party's December 1997 conference. He hoped that Ramaphosa would succeed him, believing Mbeki to be too inflexible and intolerant of criticism, but the ANC elected Mbeki regardless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006211Meredith2010568Sampson2011537–543_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006211Meredith2010568Sampson2011537–543-312">[311]</a></sup> Mandela and the Executive supported <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Zuma" title="Jacob Zuma">Jacob Zuma</a>, a Zulu who had been imprisoned on Robben Island, as Mbeki's replacement for Deputy President. Zuma's candidacy was challenged by Winnie, whose populist rhetoric had gained her a strong following within the party, although Zuma defeated her in a landslide victory vote at the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010568Sampson2011537–543_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010568Sampson2011537–543-313">[312]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela's relationship with Machel had intensified; in February 1998, he publicly stated that he was "in love with a remarkable lady", and under pressure from Tutu, who urged him to set an example for young people, he organised a wedding for his 80th birthday, in July that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Sampson2011549–551_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Sampson2011549–551-314">[313]</a></sup> The following day, he held a grand party with many foreign dignitaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011551–552_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011551–552-315">[314]</a></sup> Although the 1996 constitution allowed the president to serve two consecutive five-year terms, Mandela had never planned to stand for a second term in office. He gave his farewell speech to Parliament on 29 March 1999 when it adjourned prior to the 1999 general elections, after which he retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011578_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011578-316">[315]</a></sup> Although opinion polls in South Africa showed wavering support for both the ANC and the government, Mandela himself remained highly popular, with 80% of South Africans polled in 1999 expressing satisfaction with his performance as president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006219_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006219-317">[316]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span></h3>
<h4><span id="Continued_activism_and_philanthropy:_1999.E2.80.932004"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Continued_activism_and_philanthropy:_1999–2004">Continued activism and philanthropy: 1999–2004</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela,_2000_(4).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg/220px-Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg/330px-Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg/440px-Nelson_Mandela%2C_2000_%284%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela,_2000_(4).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela visiting the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> in 2000</div></div></div>
<p>Retiring in June 1999, Mandela aimed to lead a quiet family life, divided between Johannesburg and Qunu. Although he set about authoring a sequel to his first autobiography, to be titled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dare_Not_Linger:_The_Presidential_Years" title="Dare Not Linger: The Presidential Years">The Presidential Years</a></i>, it remained unfinished and was only published posthumously in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Battersby2011587–588_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Battersby2011587–588-318">[317]</a></sup> Mandela found such seclusion difficult and reverted to a busy public life involving a daily programme of tasks, meetings with world leaders and celebrities, and—when in Johannesburg—working with the Nelson Mandela Foundation, founded in 1999 to focus on rural development, school construction, and combating HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Battersby2011588–589_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010576Battersby2011588–589-319">[318]</a></sup> Although he had been heavily criticised for failing to do enough to fight the HIV/AIDS pandemic during his presidency, he devoted much of his time to the issue following his retirement, describing it as "a war" that had killed more than "all previous wars"; affiliating himself with the <a href="/wiki/Treatment_Action_Campaign" title="Treatment Action Campaign">Treatment Action Campaign</a>, he urged Mbeki's government to ensure that HIV-positive South Africans had access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-retrovirals" title="Anti-retrovirals">anti-retrovirals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006219–220Meredith2010584–586Battersby2011590–591_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006219–220Meredith2010584–586Battersby2011590–591-320">[319]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Mandela was successfully treated for <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a> in July 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011598_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011598-321">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[321]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, Mandela inaugurated the Nelson Mandela Annual Lecture, and in 2003 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandela_Rhodes_Foundation" title="Mandela Rhodes Foundation">Mandela Rhodes Foundation</a> was created at <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_House" title="Rhodes House">Rhodes House</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, to provide postgraduate scholarships to African students. These projects were followed by the Nelson Mandela Centre of Memory and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/46664_(concerts)" title="46664 (concerts)">46664 campaign</a> against HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011589–590_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011589–590-323">[322]</a></sup> He gave the closing address at the <a href="/wiki/XIII_International_AIDS_Conference,_2000" title="XIII International AIDS Conference, 2000">XIII International AIDS Conference</a> in Durban in 2000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[323]</a></sup> and in 2004, spoke at the <a href="/wiki/XV_International_AIDS_Conference,_2004" title="XV International AIDS Conference, 2004">XV International AIDS Conference</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>, Thailand, calling for greater measures to tackle tuberculosis as well as HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[324]</a></sup> Mandela publicised AIDS as the cause of his son <a href="/wiki/Makgatho_Mandela" title="Makgatho Mandela">Makgatho</a>'s death in January 2005, to defy the stigma about discussing the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[325]</a></sup>
</p><p>Publicly, Mandela became more vocal in criticising Western powers. He strongly opposed the 1999 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Kosovo" title="War in Kosovo">NATO intervention in Kosovo</a> and called it an attempt by the world's powerful nations to police the entire world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[326]</a></sup> In 2003, he spoke out against the plans for the United States to launch a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Iraq" title="War in Iraq">war in Iraq</a>, describing it as "a tragedy" and lambasting US president <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> and British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> (whom he referred to as an "American foreign minister") for undermining the UN, saying, "All that (Mr. Bush) wants is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraqi_oil" title="Iraqi oil">Iraqi oil</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[327]</a></sup> He attacked the United States more generally, asserting that "If there is a country that has committed unspeakable atrocities in the world, it is the United States of America", citing the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombing of Japan</a>; this attracted international controversy, although he later improved his relationship with Bush.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011591–592_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011591–592-329">[328]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[329]</a></sup> Retaining an interest in the Lockerbie suspect, he visited Megrahi in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barlinnie_prison" title="Barlinnie prison">Barlinnie prison</a> and spoke out against the conditions of his treatment, referring to them as "psychological persecution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011593_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011593-331">[330]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Retiring_from_retirement.22:_2004.E2.80.932013"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Retiring_from_retirement":_2004–2013'>"Retiring from retirement": 2004–2013</span></h4>
<p><span class="anchor" id=""></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg/220px-George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg/330px-George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg/440px-George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_W._Bush_and_Nelson_Mandela_-_walking_-_Oval_Office_-_May_17_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nelson Mandela and President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>, May 2005</div></div></div>
<p>In June 2004, aged 85 and amid failing health, Mandela announced that he was "retiring from retirement" and retreating from public life, remarking, "Don't call me, I will call you."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010593Battersby2011598_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010593Battersby2011598-332">[331]</a></sup> Although continuing to meet with close friends and family, the foundation discouraged invitations for him to appear at public events and denied most interview requests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011598_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011598-321">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>He retained some involvement in international affairs. In 2005, he founded the Nelson Mandela Legacy Trust,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-333">[332]</a></sup> travelling to the United States to speak before the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP">NAACP</a> on the need for economic assistance to Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_333-1"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-333">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAT-334">[333]</a></sup> He spoke with US senator <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> and President George W. Bush and first met the then-senator <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT_334-1"><a href="#cite_note-LAT-334">[333]</a></sup> Mandela also encouraged Zimbabwean president <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> to resign over growing <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Zimbabwe" title="Human rights in Zimbabwe">human rights</a> abuses in the country. When this proved ineffective, he spoke out publicly against Mugabe in 2007, asking him to step down "with residual respect and a modicum of dignity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011594_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011594-335">[334]</a></sup> That year, Mandela, Machel and Desmond Tutu convened a group of world leaders in Johannesburg to contribute their wisdom and independent leadership to some of the world's toughest problems. Mandela announced the formation of this new group, <a href="/wiki/The_Elders_(organization)" title="The Elders (organization)">The Elders</a>, in a speech delivered on his 89th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[335]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_-_full_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1162" data-file-width="1743" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Nelson_Mandela_-_full_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_-_full_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela receiving the freedom of the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tshwane" title="Tshwane">Tshwane</a>, 2008</div></div></div>
<p>Mandela's 90th birthday was marked across the country on 18 July 2008, with the main celebrations held at Qunu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOXNews_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOXNews-337">[336]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela_90th_Birthday_Tribute" title="Nelson Mandela 90th Birthday Tribute">concert in his honour</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[337]</a></sup> In a speech marking the event, Mandela called for the rich to help the poor across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOXNews_337-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOXNews-337">[336]</a></sup> Throughout Mbeki's presidency, Mandela continued to support the ANC, usually overshadowing Mbeki at any public events that the two attended. Mandela was more at ease with Mbeki's successor, Zuma,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010598Battersby2011594–597_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010598Battersby2011594–597-339">[338]</a></sup> although the Nelson Mandela Foundation was upset when his grandson, <a href="/wiki/Mandla_Mandela" title="Mandla Mandela">Mandla Mandela</a>, flew him out to the Eastern Cape to attend a pro-Zuma rally in the midst of a storm in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010598Battersby2011594–597_339-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010598Battersby2011594–597-339">[338]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, Mandela successfully campaigned for South Africa to host the <a href="/wiki/2010_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2010 FIFA World Cup">2010 FIFA World Cup</a>, declaring that there would be "few better gifts for us" in the year marking a decade since the fall of apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[339]</a></sup> Despite maintaining a low profile during the event due to ill health, Mandela made his final public appearance during the World Cup closing ceremony, where he received much applause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011600_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011600-341">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[341]</a></sup> Between 2005 and 2013, Mandela, and later his family, were embroiled in a series of legal disputes regarding money held in family trusts for the benefit of his descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[342]</a></sup> In mid-2013, as Mandela was hospitalised for a lung infection in Pretoria, his descendants were involved in an intra-family legal dispute relating to the burial place of Mandela's children, and ultimately Mandela himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[343]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Illness_and_death:_2011.E2.80.932013"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_death:_2011–2013">Illness and death: 2011–2013</span></h4>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Death"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_Nelson_Mandela" title="Death of Nelson Mandela">Death of Nelson Mandela</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg/220px-Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg/330px-Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg/440px-Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madiba%27s_house_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the public paying their respects outside Mandela's Houghton home</div></div></div>
<p>In February 2011, Mandela was briefly hospitalised with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_infection" title="Respiratory infection">respiratory infection</a>, attracting international attention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011607_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011607-345">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[345]</a></sup> before being re-admitted for a lung infection and <a href="/wiki/Gallstone" title="Gallstone">gallstone</a> removal in December 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[347]</a></sup> After a successful medical procedure in early March 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[348]</a></sup> his lung infection recurred and he was briefly hospitalised in Pretoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[349]</a></sup> In June 2013, his lung infection worsened and he was readmitted to a Pretoria hospital in serious condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[350]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Diocese_of_Cape_Town" title="Anglican Diocese of Cape Town">Archbishop of Cape Town</a> <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Makgoba" title="Thabo Makgoba">Thabo Makgoba</a> visited Mandela at the hospital and prayed with Machel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[351]</a></sup> while Zuma cancelled a trip to Mozambique to visit him the following day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[352]</a></sup> In September 2013, Mandela was discharged from hospital,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[353]</a></sup> although his condition remained unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[354]</a></sup>
</p><p>After suffering from a prolonged respiratory infection, Mandela died on 5 December 2013 at the age of 95, at around 20:50 <a href="/wiki/South_African_Standard_Time" title="South African Standard Time">local time</a> at his home in Houghton, surrounded by his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCD_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCD-356">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[356]</a></sup> Zuma publicly announced his death on television,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCD_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCD-356">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[357]</a></sup> proclaiming ten days of national mourning, a memorial service held at Johannesburg's <a href="/wiki/FNB_Stadium" title="FNB Stadium">FNB Stadium</a> on 10 December 2013, and 8 December as a national day of prayer and reflection. Mandela's body <a href="/wiki/Lying_in_state" title="Lying in state">lay in state</a> from 11 to 13 December at the <a href="/wiki/Union_Buildings" title="Union Buildings">Union Buildings</a> in Pretoria and a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a> was held on 15 December in Qunu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[358]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[359]</a></sup> Approximately 90 representatives of foreign states travelled to South Africa to attend memorial events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[360]</a></sup> It was later revealed that 300 million rand (about 20 million dollars) originally earmarked for humanitarian development projects had been redirected to finance the funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[361]</a></sup> The media was awash with tributes and reminiscences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014917_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014917-363">[362]</a></sup> while images of tributes to Mandela proliferated across social media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2014130_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2014130-364">[363]</a></sup> His US$4.1 million estate was left to his widow, other family members, staff, and educational institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[364]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_ideology">Political ideology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>A friend once asked me how I could reconcile my creed of African nationalism with a belief in dialectical materialism. For me, there was no contradiction. I was first and foremost an African nationalist fighting for our emancipation from minority rule and the right to control our own destiny. But at the same time, South Africa and the African continent were part of the larger world. Our problems, while distinctive and special, were not unique, and a philosophy that placed those problems in an international and historical context of the greater world and the course of history was valuable. I was prepared to use whatever means necessary to speed up the erasure of human prejudice and the end of chauvinistic and violent nationalism.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Nelson Mandela, 1994<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994173_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994173-366">[365]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Mandela identified as both an <a href="/wiki/African_nationalism" title="African nationalism">African nationalist</a>, an ideological position he held since joining the ANC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198625,_232Lodge2006220Meredith2010241Sampson201137,_584_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198625,_232Lodge2006220Meredith2010241Sampson201137,_584-367">[366]</a></sup> and as a socialist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986231–232Smith2010231_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986231–232Smith2010231-368">[367]</a></sup> He was a practical politician, rather than an intellectual scholar or political theorist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer200813Barnard201414_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer200813Barnard201414-369">[368]</a></sup> According to biographer Tom Lodge, "for Mandela, politics has always been primarily about enacting stories, about making narratives, primarily about morally exemplary conduct, and only secondarily about ideological vision, more about means rather than ends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006ix_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006ix-370">[369]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Sabelo_J._Ndlovu-Gatsheni" title="Sabelo J. Ndlovu-Gatsheni">Sabelo J. Ndlovu-Gatsheni</a> described Mandela as a "liberal African nationalist–decolonial humanist",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907_371-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907-371">[370]</a></sup> while political analyst <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Suttner" title="Raymond Suttner">Raymond Suttner</a> cautioned against labelling Mandela a liberal and stated that Mandela displayed a "hybrid socio-political make-up".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuttner2007122_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuttner2007122-372">[371]</a></sup> Mandela adopted some of his political ideas from other thinkers—among them Indian independence leaders like Gandhi and Nehru, African-American civil rights activists, and African nationalists like <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Nkrumah</a>—and applied them to the South African situation. At the same time he rejected other aspects of their thought, such as the anti-white sentiment of many African nationalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008109_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008109-373">[372]</a></sup> In doing so he synthesised both counter-cultural and hegemonic views, for instance by drawing upon ideas from the then-dominant <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner_nationalism" title="Afrikaner nationalism">Afrikaner nationalism</a> in promoting his anti-apartheid vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008102_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008102-374">[373]</a></sup>
</p><p>His political development was strongly influenced by his legal training and practice, in particular his hope to achieve change not through violence but through "legal revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006viii_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006viii-375">[374]</a></sup> Over the course of his life, he began by advocating a path of non-violence, later embracing violence, and then adopting a non-violent approach to negotiation and reconciliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008107_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008107-376">[375]</a></sup> When endorsing violence, he did so because he saw no alternative, and was always pragmatic about it, perceiving it as a means to get his opponent to the negotiating table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008105,_108_377-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008105,_108-377">[376]</a></sup> He sought to target symbols of white supremacy and racist oppression rather than white people as individuals, and was anxious not to inaugurate a race war in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014914_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014914-378">[377]</a></sup> This willingness to use violence distinguishes Mandela from the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Gandhism" title="Gandhism">Gandhism</a>, with which some commentators have sought to associate him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuttner2007119–120_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuttner2007119–120-379">[378]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democracy">Democracy</span></h3>
<p>Although he presented himself in an autocratic manner in several speeches, Mandela was a devout believer in democracy and abided by majority decisions even when deeply disagreeing with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011433_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011433-380">[379]</a></sup> He had exhibited a commitment to the values of democracy and human rights since at least the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014906–907_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014906–907-381">[380]</a></sup> He held a conviction that "inclusivity, accountability and freedom of speech" were the fundamentals of democracy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011605_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011605-382">[381]</a></sup> and was driven by a belief in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" title="Natural and legal rights">natural</a> and human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKalumba1995162_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKalumba1995162-383">[382]</a></sup> Suttner argued that there were "two modes of leadership" that Mandela adopted. On one side he adhered to ideas about collective leadership, although on the other believed that there were scenarios in which a leader had to be decisive and act without consultation to achieve a particular objective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuttner2007113–114_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuttner2007113–114-384">[383]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Lodge, Mandela's political thought reflected tensions between his support for <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a> and pre-colonial African forms of consensus decision making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006xi_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006xi-385">[384]</a></sup> He was an admirer of British-style <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907_371-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907-371">[370]</a></sup> stating that "I regard the British Parliament as the most democratic institution in the world, and the independence and impartiality of its judiciary never fail to arouse my admiration."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907_371-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907-371">[370]</a></sup> In this he has been described as being committed to "the Euro-North American modernist project of emancipation", something which distinguishes him from other African nationalist and socialist leaders like Nyerere who were concerned about embracing styles of democratic governance that were Western, rather than African, in origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907_371-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907-371">[370]</a></sup> Mandela nevertheless also expressed admiration for what he deemed to be indigenous forms of democracy, describing Xhosa traditional society's mode of governance as "democracy in its purest form".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907_371-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENdlovu-Gatsheni2014907-371">[370]</a></sup> He also spoke of an influential African ethical tenet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(philosophy)" title="Ubuntu (philosophy)">Ubuntu</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Nguni_languages" title="Nguni languages">Ngnuni term</a> meaning "A person is a person through other persons" or "I am because we are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOppenheim2012369–370_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOppenheim2012369–370-386">[385]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialism_and_Marxism">Socialism and Marxism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Soviet_Union_1988_CPA_5971_stamp_(70th_birth_anniversary_of_Nelson_Mandela,_South_African_anti-apartheid_revolutionary,_political_leader_and_philanthropist).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/The_Soviet_Union_1988_CPA_5971_stamp_%2870th_birth_anniversary_of_Nelson_Mandela%2C_South_African_anti-apartheid_revolutionary%2C_political_leader_and_philanthropist%29.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/The_Soviet_Union_1988_CPA_5971_stamp_%2870th_birth_anniversary_of_Nelson_Mandela%2C_South_African_anti-apartheid_revolutionary%2C_political_leader_and_philanthropist%29.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/The_Soviet_Union_1988_CPA_5971_stamp_%2870th_birth_anniversary_of_Nelson_Mandela%2C_South_African_anti-apartheid_revolutionary%2C_political_leader_and_philanthropist%29.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Soviet_Union_1988_CPA_5971_stamp_(70th_birth_anniversary_of_Nelson_Mandela,_South_African_anti-apartheid_revolutionary,_political_leader_and_philanthropist).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1988 Soviet commemorative stamp, captioned "The fighter for freedom of South Africa Nelson Mandela" in Russian</div></div></div>
<p>Mandela advocated the ultimate establishment of a classless society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986231,_232Ellis20167_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986231,_232Ellis20167-387">[386]</a></sup> with Sampson describing him as being "openly opposed to capitalism, private land-ownership and the power of big money".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011298_388-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011298-388">[387]</a></sup> Mandela was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, and during the revolution he advocated <a href="/wiki/Scientific_socialism" title="Scientific socialism">scientific socialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011282_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011282-389">[388]</a></sup> He denied being a communist at the Treason Trial,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994365Sampson2011135–138_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994365Sampson2011135–138-390">[389]</a></sup> and maintained this stance both when later talking to journalists,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson1986232_391-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson1986232-391">[390]</a></sup> and in his autobiography, where he outlined that the cooperation with the SACP was pragmatic, asking rhetorically, "who is to say that we were not using them?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis201618_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis201618-392">[391]</a></sup> According to the sociologist Craig Soudien, "sympathetic as Mandela was to socialism, a communist he was not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoudien2015361_393-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoudien2015361-393">[392]</a></sup> Conversely, the biographer David Jones Smith stated that Mandela "embraced communism and communists" in the late 1950s and early 1960s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2010217–218_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2010217–218-394">[393]</a></sup> while the historian Stephen Ellis commented that Mandela had assimilated much of the <a href="/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism" title="Marxism–Leninism">Marxist–Leninist</a> ideology by 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis20167_395-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis20167-395">[394]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ellis also found evidence that Mandela had been an active member of the South African Communist Party during the late 1950s and early 1960s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2011667–668_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2011667–668-120">[119]</a></sup> something that was confirmed after his death by both the ANC and the SACP, the latter of which claimed that he was not only a member of the party, but also served on its Central Committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandela'sCommunism_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela'sCommunism-122">[121]</a></sup> His membership had been hidden by the ANC, aware that knowledge of Mandela's former SACP involvement might have been detrimental to his attempts to attract support from Western countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis201617_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis201617-396">[395]</a></sup> Mandela's view of these Western governments differed from those of Marxist–Leninists, for he did not believe that they were anti-democratic or reactionary and remained committed to democratic systems of governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2010231_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2010231-397">[396]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1955 Freedom Charter, which Mandela had helped create, called for the nationalisation of banks, gold mines and land, to ensure equal distribution of wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKalumba1995164–165_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKalumba1995164–165-398">[397]</a></sup> Despite these beliefs, Mandela initiated a programme of privatisation during his presidency in line with trends in other countries of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreund2014294_399-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreund2014294-399">[398]</a></sup> It has been repeatedly suggested that Mandela would have preferred to develop a <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democratic</a> economy in South Africa but that this was not feasible as a result of the international political and economic situation during the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreund2014294_399-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreund2014294-399">[398]</a></sup> This decision was in part influenced by the fall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_states" title="Socialist states">socialist states</a> in the Soviet Union and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> during the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011433–435_400-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011433–435-400">[399]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality_and_personal_life">Personality and personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="416" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Nelson_Mandela_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Nelson_Mandela_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Nelson_Mandela_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Nelson_Mandela_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Nelson_Mandela_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Mandela_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mandela on a visit to Australia in 2009; he is wearing one of the brightly coloured garments that became known as "<a href="/wiki/Madiba_shirt" title="Madiba shirt">Madiba shirts</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>Mandela was widely considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charismatic_leader" title="Charismatic leader">charismatic leader</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997570Read2010326_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997570Read2010326-401">[400]</a></sup> described by biographer <a href="/wiki/Mary_Benson_(campaigner)" title="Mary Benson (campaigner)">Mary Benson</a> as "a born mass leader who could not help magnetizing people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198651_402-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198651-402">[401]</a></sup> He was highly image conscious and throughout his life always sought out fine quality clothes, with many commentators believing that he carried himself in a regal manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011432,_554_403-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011432,_554-403">[402]</a></sup> His aristocratic heritage was repeatedly emphasised by supporters, thus contributing to his "charismatic power".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge20062_404-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge20062-404">[403]</a></sup> While living in Johannesburg in the 1950s, he cultivated the image of the "African gentleman", having "the pressed clothes, correct manners, and modulated public speech" associated with such a position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008111_405-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008111-405">[404]</a></sup> In doing so, Lodge argued that Mandela became "one of the first media politicians ... embodying a glamour and a style that projected <i>visually</i> a brave new African world of modernity and freedom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge2006ix_370-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge2006ix-370">[369]</a></sup> Mandela was known to change his clothes several times a day, and he became so associated with highly coloured <a href="/wiki/Batik" title="Batik">Batik</a> shirts after assuming the presidency that they came to be known as "<a href="/wiki/Madiba_shirt" title="Madiba shirt">Madiba shirts</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008133Meredith2010495Sampson2011503_406-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008133Meredith2010495Sampson2011503-406">[405]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-407"><a href="#cite_note-407">[406]</a></sup>
</p><p>For political scientists <a href="/wiki/Betty_Glad" title="Betty Glad">Betty Glad</a> and Robert Blanton, Mandela was an "exceptionally intelligent, shrewd, and loyal leader".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997577_408-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997577-408">[407]</a></sup> His official biographer, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Sampson" title="Anthony Sampson">Anthony Sampson</a>, commented that he was a "master of imagery and performance", excelling at presenting himself well in press photographs and producing sound bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011582–583_409-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011582–583-409">[408]</a></sup> His public speeches were presented in a formal, stiff manner, and often consisted of clichéd set phrases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008128,_134_410-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008128,_134-410">[409]</a></sup> He typically spoke slowly, and carefully chose his words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997576_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997576-411">[410]</a></sup> Although he was not considered a great orator, his speeches conveyed "his personal commitment, charm and humour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber200487_412-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber200487-412">[411]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela was a private person who often concealed his emotions and confided in very few people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuttner2007119Meredith2010xv–xvi_413-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuttner2007119Meredith2010xv–xvi-413">[412]</a></sup> Privately, he lived an austere life, refusing to drink alcohol or smoke, and even as president made his own bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xvi_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xvi-414">[413]</a></sup> Renowned for his mischievous sense of humour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011599_415-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattersby2011599-415">[414]</a></sup> he was known for being both stubborn and loyal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xviSampson2011583_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xviSampson2011583-416">[415]</a></sup> and at times exhibited a quick temper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997582Meredith2010xvi_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997582Meredith2010xvi-417">[416]</a></sup> He was typically friendly and welcoming, and appeared relaxed in conversation with everyone, including his opponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011411,_498_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011411,_498-418">[417]</a></sup> A self-described <a href="/wiki/Anglophile" title="Anglophile">Anglophile</a>, he claimed to have lived by the "trappings of British style and manners".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997581Ndlovu-Gatsheni2014907_419-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGladBlanton1997581Ndlovu-Gatsheni2014907-419">[418]</a></sup> Constantly polite and courteous, he was attentive to all, irrespective of their age or status, and often talked to children or servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xvi,_482–483_420-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010xvi,_482–483-420">[419]</a></sup> He was known for his ability to find common ground with very different communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard20145–6_421-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard20145–6-421">[420]</a></sup> In later life, he always looked for the best in people, even defending political opponents to his allies, who sometimes thought him too trusting of others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson2011431,_582_422-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson2011431,_582-422">[421]</a></sup> He was fond of <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian cuisine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010164_423-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010164-423">[422]</a></sup> and had a lifelong interest in archaeology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer1988189_424-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer1988189-424">[423]</a></sup> and boxing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodge200629_425-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodge200629-425">[424]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The significance of Mandela can be considered in two related ways. First, he has provided through his personal presence as a benign and honest conviction politician, skilled at exerting power but not obsessed with it to the point of view of excluding principles, a man who struggled to display respect to all ... Second, in so doing he was able to be a hero and a symbol to an array of otherwise unlikely mates through his ability, like all brilliant nationalist politicians, to speak to very different audiences effectively at once.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Bill_Freund_(historian)" title="Bill Freund (historian)">Bill Freund</a>, academic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreund2014295_426-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreund2014295-426">[425]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>He was raised in the Methodist denomination of Christianity; the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church_of_Southern_Africa" title="Methodist Church of Southern Africa">Methodist Church of Southern Africa</a> claimed that he retained his allegiance to them throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster201489_427-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster201489-427">[426]</a></sup> On analysing Mandela's writings, the theologian <a href="/wiki/Dion_Forster" title="Dion Forster">Dion Forster</a> described him as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_humanist" title="Christian humanist">Christian humanist</a>, although added that his thought relied to a greater extent on the Southern African concept of <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_philosophy" title="Ubuntu philosophy">Ubuntu</a> than on <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster2014106–107_428-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster2014106–107-428">[427]</a></sup> According to Sampson, Mandela never had "a strong religious faith" however,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampson201165_429-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampson201165-429">[428]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Elleke_Boehmer" title="Elleke Boehmer">Elleke Boehmer</a> stated that Mandela's religious belief was "never robust".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer200886_430-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer200886-430">[429]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mandela was very self-conscious about being a man and regularly made references to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhood" title="Manhood">manhood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuttner2014342_431-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuttner2014342-431">[430]</a></sup> He was heterosexual,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008142_432-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoehmer2008142-432">[431]</a></sup> and biographer <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Meer" title="Fatima Meer">Fatima Meer</a> said that he was "easily tempted" by women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeer198878_433-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeer198878-433">[432]</a></sup> Another biographer, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Meredith" title="Martin Meredith">Martin Meredith</a>, characterised him as being "by nature a romantic", highlighting that he had relationships with various women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith201021_434-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith201021-434">[433]</a></sup> Mandela was married three times, fathered six children, and had seventeen grandchildren and at least seventeen great-grandchildren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genealogy_435-0"><a href="#cite_note-genealogy-435">[434]</a></sup> He could be stern and demanding of his children, although he was more affectionate with his grandchildren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010481Smith2010147Sampson2011246_436-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeredith2010481Smith2010147Sampson2011246-436">[435]</a></sup> His first marriage was to Evelyn Ntoko Mase in October 1944;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994144,_148–149Smith201059–62Sampson201136_437-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994144,_148–149Smith201059–62Sampson201136-437">[436]</a></sup> they divorced in March 1958 under the multiple strains of his adultery and constant absences, devotion to revolutionary agitation, and the fact that she was a Jehovah's Witness, a religion requiring political neutrality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMandela1994296Smith2010102–104Sampson2011110_438-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMandela1994296Smith2010102–104Sampson2011110-438">[437]</a></sup> Mandela's second wife was the social worker Winnie Madikizela-Mandela, whom he married in June 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson198674–76Meer198893Mandela1994306–311Meredith2010144–149Smith2010104,_132–145Sampson2011110–113_439-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson198674–76Meer198893Mandela1994306–311Meredith2010144–149Smith2010104,_132–145Sampson2011110–113-439">[438]</a></sup> They divorced in March 1996.<sup class="reference"
